At Least Two People Killed In Kyiv After Early Morning Strikes Hit Residential Buildings

At least two people were killed in Kyiv after residential buildings in Kyiv were hit by multiple strikes early on Tuesday morning, Ukraine’s state emergency services said[https://www.facebook.com/MNS.GOV.UA/posts/332970388870857].

In Kyiv’s western district of Sviatoshynskyi, a nine-story apartment building and a 16-story residential building were hit by shelling sometime after 5 a.m. local time. The nine story building only suffered minor damage with fires being quickly put out. But two people in the larger building were killed by the strike while 35 others were rescued.

At around 6 a.m., a 10-story apartment building in the city’s central Podilskyi District was also struck by shelling, causing a fire on the first five floors. A two-floor private home in Osokorky district in the southern part of the city was also hit by shelling.

War Could Be Over By May As Russia Runs Out Of Resources, Ukrainian Officials Claim

The war could end by early May when the Russian military runs out of necessary resources to continue its invasion of Ukraine, Oleksiy Arestovich, adviser to the Ukrainian president’s chief of staff, claimed on Monday.

According to[https://www.reuters.com/world/europe/war-could-be-over-by-may-says-ukrainian-presidential-adviser-2022-03-15/] Reuters, Arestovich said an end date depends on the resources the Kremlin puts into the invasion, adding: “I think that no later than in May, early May, we should have a peace agreement, maybe much earlier, we will see, I am talking about the latest possible dates.”

Arestovich, who has not directly been involved in peace talks, said either a peace deal would be worked out within a week or two—with Russia withdrawing its troops, or there will be attempts to bring in Syrian fighters for “round two” who will also be ground down, followed by an agreement by mid or late April. Another possibility could see conscripts with little training sent to fight, he added.

The basis of Arestovich’s claims remain unclear but questions about Russia’s ability to support a long drawn out campaign has been questioned by some experts.

This push comes amid protests by the region’s residents on Monday, who the Russian forces tried to disperse by firing warning shots. Multiple protests have also taken place in other Russian occupied regions including Melitopol and Berdyansk, the report adds.

The update warns that Russia may engage in further attempts to subvert Ukrainian democracy as it attempts to “consolidate political control of Ukraine.”

Zelensky Thanks Russians Protesting Invasion—Including TV Staffer Who Held Antiwar Sign

Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky thanked Russian citizens who have protested their country’s invasion of Ukraine in a speech late Monday night.

“I am grateful to those Russians who do not stop trying to convey the truth,” Zelensky said, according to a translation by CNN[https://www.cnn.com/europe/live-news/ukraine-russia-putin-news-03-14-22/index.html].

Zelensky specifically thanked Marina Ovysannikova, an editor at state television network Russia Channel One who held up a sign during a news broadcast Monday that read, “NO WAR. Stop the war. Do not believe propaganda they tell you lies here.”

Russia has sought to control how its invasion of Ukraine is covered in local news. Some of the country’s only independent news outlets have shut down, and a law passed earlier this month[https://www.reuters.com/world/europe/russia-introduce-jail-terms-spreading-fake-information-about-army-2022-03-04/] threatens prison terms of up to 15 years for spreading military information the Russian government deems to be “fake.” Some residents of cities like Moscow and St. Petersburg have protested the war, but OVD-info—an independent human rights group— estimates[https://ovdinfo.org/] Russian authorities have detained nearly 15,000 people at antiwar demonstrations since the invasion began.

Ukrainian officials estimate[https://www.cnn.com/europe/live-news/ukraine-russia-putin-news-03-14-22/h_3db1542de366d188c4e7979e7b5ecc95] more than 2,500 people have died in Mariupol since the siege began, though independent third parties have yet to verify those figures.

March 14, 2022 11:07 PM EST

Russia Reportedly Requested Surface-To-Air Missiles, Drones From China—US Has ‘Deep Concerns’

Following Sunday’s reports[https://www.nytimes.com/2022/03/13/us/politics/russia-china-ukraine.html] that Russia requested military and economic assistance from China, the Financial Times on Monday detailed[https://www.ft.com/content/52ea7aab-f8d1-46b6-9d66-18545c5ef9b9] the types of weapons Russia requested. Unnamed officials said Russia requested surface-to-air missiles, drones, armored vehicles, intelligence-related equipment and vehicles for logistics support—and that China had signaled its willingness to provide the equipment, according to the report.

The news follows U.S. National Security Advisor Jake Sullivan’s Monday meeting with China’s top diplomat, Yang Jiechi, in Rome. The meeting lasted seven hours and covered a wide range of topics, including China’s relationship with Russia, according to a senior U.S. administration official. The official said the U.S. has “deep concerns about China's alignment with Russia at this time,” and that Sullivan expressed these concerns to Jiechi, along with the “consequences of certain actions.”

March 14, 2022 6:30 PM EST

Ukraine Will Fall Into ‘Deep Recession’: IMF

The International Monetary Fund said it expects Ukraine to enter a “deep recession” due to Russia’s invasion of the country, in a report[https://www.imf.org/en/Publications/CR/Issues/2022/03/10/Ukraine-Request-for-Purchase-under-the-Rapid-Financing-Instrument-and-Cancellation-of-Stand-514148] released Monday. The IMF said Ukraine’s GDP will fall this year 10% “at a minimum” and Ukraine’s real GDP may eventually contract by as much as 35%, based on analysis using data from other recently wartorn countries.

The report was prepared March 7 ahead of the IMF’s approval[https://www.reuters.com/world/europe/imf-approves-14-bln-emergency-funding-ukraine-2022-03-09/] of $1.4 billion in financing to Ukraine. The $1.4 billion loan is the most the IMF can directly give to Ukraine in funding and the IMF is in the process of making a trust fund for Ukraine that other countries can donate to, Reuters reported[https://www.reuters.com/world/imf-says-working-bilateral-donor-trust-fund-ukraine-2022-03-14/] Monday, citing an anonymous IMF official.

WHO Outlines Plans To Deliver Medical Supplies To Ukraine, Condemns Attacks On Health Care

The World Health Organization on Monday said it was coordinating the delivery of critical medical equipment and drugs to Ukraine as its healthcare system grapples with supply shortages.

In a statement[https://www.euro.who.int/en/media-centre/sections/press-releases/2022/who-is-working-day-and-night-to-keep-medical-supply-chains-open-and-preserve-ukraines-health-system], the WHO said that a total of 18 million people have been affected by the Russian invasion, including 6.7 million who have been internally displaced and 3 million refugees who have left the country.

The global agency has set up a hub in neighboring Poland to support operations into Ukraine and stepped up surveillance of possible disease outbreaks in the country. The relief mission is being carried out in coordination with Ukraine’s health ministry.

“The coming days and weeks will see a constant flow of medical supplies, as part of an effort to ensure people’s access to essential drugs and medical care.”— WHO Statement

The WHO also condemned attacks on medical facilities inside Ukraine, saying: “Every single attack deprives people of life-saving services. Attacks on health care are violations of international humanitarian law and human rights.”

Renaud was a Peabody Award-winning filmmaker, producing humanitarian documentaries across several continents alongside his brother, Craig Renaud. The Times said[https://twitter.com/cliffordlevy/status/1502994874068762629/photo/1] he last worked for the publication in 2015, though used a Times press badge in his Ukraine coverage.

Jane Ferguson, a contributor to PBS and the New Yorker, said[https://twitter.com/janeferguson5/status/1503000669153808387?s=21] a Ukrainian police officer told her at the site where Renaud was killed: “Tell America, tell the world, what they did to a journalist,” indicating Russian forces killed Renaud.

March 12, 2022 11:29 PM EST

Ukraine Says Russia Seeking To Take Permanent Control Of Zaporizhzhia Nuclear Power Plant

Ukraine believes Russia is taking steps to seize “full and permanent” control of the Zaporizhzhia nuclear power plant, where around 400 Russian soldiers are present, the International Atomic Energy Agency said in a statement[https://www.iaea.org/newscenter/pressreleases/update-19-iaea-director-general-statement-on-situation-in-ukraine] Saturday, adding that Russian authorities denied the accusation.

Energoatom, Ukraine’s state-run nuclear energy company, told the IAEA that Russia plans to hand over the management of Europe’s largest nuclear power plant, which its troops seized last week, to Russia’s state-run company Rosatom, the statement said.

Rosatom Director General Alexei Likhachev denied to the IAEA that it had any plans to take over management of the power plant but admitted Russian experts were at the plant to provide “consultative assistance,” the statement said.

Energoatom said Saturday morning that one of the 11 Rosatom officials who arrived at the plant Friday claimed the plant belonged to Russia, CNN reported[https://www.cnn.com/europe/live-news/ukraine-russia-putin-news-03-12-22/h_58411049df7971c8fd14eaee4aa4f910].

Russian military forces captured the plant March 4, which has six reactors, and Energoatom has said its employees have since been working at “gunpoint,” CNN reported[https://www.cnn.com/europe/live-news/ukraine-russia-putin-news-03-12-22/h_58411049df7971c8fd14eaee4aa4f910].

Two of the four high voltage offsite power lines have been damaged but a functioning power line can provide the power the plant needs, the IAEAsaid.

Moscow Stock Exchange To Stay Closed For Third Week

The Russian Central Bank said the Moscow Stock Exchange will remain closed next week through March 18, marking the third week in a row that trading will be halted on the country’s main stock market. The central bank said foreign exchange transactions will be allowed to resume Monday morning.

The Moscow Exchange hasn’t opened since February 25, one day after the Russian invasion[https://www.forbes.com/sites/joewalsh/2022/02/23/russia-orders-special-military-operation-in-ukraine/] into Ukraine began, and days before several Western countries imposed[https://www.forbes.com/sites/lisakim/2022/02/26/us-and-allies-to-remove-some-russian-banks-from-swift-sanction-central-bank/] harsh economic sanctions on Russia’s economy. March 12, 2022 6:03 PM EST

Ukrainian Civilian Casualties Rise Over 1,500, U.N. Says

Russia’s invasion into Ukraine has caused 1,581 casualties since the war began, the United Nations said[https://www.ohchr.org/EN/NewsEvents/Pages/DisplayNews.aspx?NewsID=28273&LangID=E] Saturday, though the organization believes the actual figures are “considerably higher.” The U.N.’s Office of the High Commissioner for Human Rights said 579 civilians had been killed, including 42 children, and 1,002 had been injured.

The U.N. office said most of the casualties had been caused by “explosive weapons with a wide impact area,” including shelling and missile strikes. The organization cited delayed information from areas with intense fighting and reports pending corroboration as reasons why its figures may be an underestimate. It noted reports from the Prosecutor General’s Office of Ukraine, which alleged[https://twitter.com/KyivIndependent/status/1502574444493127687?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw] 79 children had been killed, and allegations of hundreds of casualties in the cities of Izium, Mariupol and Volnovakha. The figures released Saturday do not include casualty statistics from those areas.

March 12, 2022 5:00 PM EST

White House Authorizes Another $200 Million In Military Aid For Ukraine

President Joe Biden authorized[https://www.whitehouse.gov/briefing-room/presidential-actions/2022/03/12/memorandum-on-the-delegation-of-authority-under-section-506a1-of-the-foreign-assistance-act-of-1961/] a $200 million aid package for Ukraine on Saturday that will provide defense articles and services, and military education and training. In a memo[https://www.whitehouse.gov/briefing-room/presidential-actions/2022/03/12/memorandum-on-the-delegation-of-authority-under-section-506a1-of-the-foreign-assistance-act-of-1961/] released by the White House, Biden delegated Secretary of State Antony Blinken to direct the funds to Ukraine as part of the Foreign Assistance Act of 1961, which allows[https://www.dsca.mil/sites/default/files/drawdown_handbook_2004b.pdf] a president to authorize a drawdown, or the “disposition of United States property or services,” to a foreign nation during an emergency.

White House officials told reporters the new aid includes more man-portable Stinger anti-aircraft missile systems and Javelin anti-tank missiles.

Earlier in the day, Russian Deputy Foreign Minister Sergei Ryabkov said that Russia could attack shipments of military aid for Ukraine, describing them as “ legitimate targets[https://www.politico.com/news/2022/03/12/russia-warns-u-s-over-arms-shipments-to-ukraine-00016820].”

This is the fourth drawdown package from the U.S. to Ukraine in the past year, and a senior U.S. defense official told reporters last week Biden’s last drawdown[https://www.state.gov/additional-military-assistance-for-ukraine/] of $350 million constituted “the largest presidential drawdown package in history.” Another senior U.S. defense official told reporters on Wednesday the U.S. is very close to delivering the $350 million aid package, and expected for it to be completely closed out “in the coming days.”

Congress also passed a $1.5 trillion spending bill[https://www.forbes.com/sites/jonathanponciano/2022/03/10/senate-passes-15-trillion-spending-bill-with-14-billion-for-ukraine-aid-heres-whats-in-it/?sh=1af1676eb713] earlier this week, which appropriated $13.6 billion in emergency aid to Ukraine to help displaced refugees, provide military assistance and for any macroeconomic needs, according[https://appropriations.house.gov/sites/democrats.appropriations.house.gov/files/Ukraine%20Fact%20Sheet.pdf] to the House Committee on Appropriations.

The Ukrainian postal service announced[https://www.ukrposhta.ua/en/news/57585-obrano-eskiz-dlja-poshtovoi-marki-russkij-voennyj-korabl-idi-nauj] the winning design of its “Russian Warship, Go F–k Yourself” stamp contest Saturday to commemorate the soldiers who reportedly cursed[https://www.politico.eu/article/go-fuck-yourself-ukraine-soldiers-snake-island-russia-war-ship-killed/] at invading Russian troops on Snake Island when they were asked to surrender. Ukraine’s First Deputy Foreign Minister Emine Dzheppar shared the design in a tweet[https://twitter.com/EmineDzheppar/status/1502545496614789126], and said the postal service will soon publish the stamp.

Ukrposhta, the country’s postal service, offered up 20 designs for a public vote[https://www.facebook.com/ukrposhta/posts/4970728059640475]. The winner was made by Boris Groh, a Ukrainian who was forced to move from his home in Crimea after Russia’s annexation of it in 2014, according to Ukrposhta[https://www.ukrposhta.ua/en/news/57585-obrano-eskiz-dlja-poshtovoi-marki-russkij-voennyj-korabl-idi-nauj].

The stamp commemorates one of the most inspiring acts of defiance in the war so far by outgunned Ukrainians. In the early days of the invasion, a Russian warship hailed a small force of 13 Ukrainian border guards on Snake Island, which is about 186 miles west of Crimea, and told them to surrender. They reportedly[https://www.washingtonpost.com/national-security/2022/02/25/snake-island-russian-warship-ukraine/] responded, “Russian warship, go f–k yourself.”

All 13 were initially thought to have been killed, leading Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky to announce[https://twitter.com/washingtonpost/status/1497187425071865870] he would posthumously award them with the Hero of Ukraine award. However, the State Border Guard Service of Ukraine later wrote in a Facebook post[https://www.facebook.com/DPSUkraine/] it has a “strong belief” they may still be alive.

The Premier League’s board said the decision does not impact the club’s “ability to train and play its fixtures.”

His disqualification comes days after the U.K. government sanctioned[https://www.forbes.com/sites/siladityaray/2022/03/10/uk-sanctions-roman-abramovich-and-six-other-russian-oligarchs-freezes-assets-including-chelsea-fc/?sh=3121ec924bbd] Abramovich and several other Russian oligarchs over their ties to Russian President Vladimir Putin. A disqualified director cannot own a club, and must put it up for sale, according to Premier League rules, however, Abramovich already announced[https://www.forbes.com/sites/annakaplan/2022/03/02/russian-billionaire-roman-abramovich-to-sell-chelsea-fcdonate-proceeds-to-help-victims-in-ukraine/] last week he intended to sell the club. The billionaire, who’s net worth[https://www.forbes.com/profile/roman-abramovich/?sh=4b20f3fb134a] is about $7.8 billion, bought the club for about $190 million in 2003. Chelsea is now valued at more than $3 billion, according to Forbes estimates[https://www.forbes.com/sites/mikeozanian/2021/04/12/the-worlds-most-valuable-soccer-teams-barcelona-on-top-at-48-billion/].

The Ukrainian Ministry of Foreign Affairs denounced the move[https://www.facebook.com/UkraineMFA/posts/335670685270070] as an “abduction” and a “war crime under the Geneva Convention.”

March 12, 2022 1:56 AM EST

Zelensky Says Over 7,000 People Fled 4 Cities Friday—Down From A Day Earlier

Some 7,144 Ukrainian civilians escaped from four cities—Enerhodar, Hostomel, Bucha and Kozarovychi—on Friday, Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky said in a late-night speech[https://www.president.gov.ua/en/news/sogodni-kozhen-zdobuvaye-ukrayini-slavu-na-svoyemu-misci-zve-73501]. It’s a steep decrease[https://www.president.gov.ua/en/news/derzhava-robit-use-shob-dopomogti-mariupolyu-zvernennya-prez-73485] from the nearly 40,000 evacuees from seven cities reported by Zelensky a day earlier, as Ukrainian officials seek to create evacuation routes in cities besieged by Russian forces.

Zelensky said “these are 7,144 reasons to try to organize evacuation for Ukrainians from the besieged cities tomorrow and the day after tomorrow,” according to a translation from his office.

Ukrainian and Russian negotiators have sought to set up[https://www.cbsnews.com/news/ukraine-russia-ceasefire-opening-several-civilian-evacuation-corridors/] temporary, localized ceasefires that would allow residents of some cities to flee via “humanitarian corridors.” However, Ukraine has accused Russia of repeatedly[https://www.reuters.com/world/europe/russia-promises-silence-ukrainians-flee-battered-cities-2022-03-09/] violating[https://www.reuters.com/world/europe/russia-ukraine-blame-each-other-mariupol-evacuation-fails-again-2022-03-06/] ceasefire agreements, and the Ukrainian government has rebuffed Russia’s proposal[https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/mar/07/russia-humanitarian-corridors-ukraine-war-mariupol-kyiv] to set up evacuation corridors that lead to Russia or Belarus[https://www.nbcnews.com/news/world/ukraine-rejects-russia-cease-fire-humanitarian-corridors-putin-belarus-rcna18936].

Zelensky also accused Russia of blocking[https://www.president.gov.ua/en/news/sogodni-kozhen-zdobuvaye-ukrayini-slavu-na-svoyemu-misci-zve-73501] aid to the southeastern port city of Mariupol, which has run low on basic supplies[https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/mar/11/ukraine-russia-war-mariupol-evacuation] amid a siege and frequent shelling[https://www.nytimes.com/2022/03/09/world/europe/ukraine-mariupol-siege.html] by Russian forces. He said the government will attempt to deliver food, water and medicine again Saturday.

March 11, 2022 10:35 PM EST

Deutsche Bank ‘Winding Down’ Business In Russia

Deutsche Bank is currently “winding down” its presence in Russia and will not do any new business in the country, it announced[https://www.db.com/news/detail/20220311-statement-on-russia?language_id=1] Friday, making it the latest bank to distance itself from Russia.

In a statement, Deutsche Bank cited “legal and regulatory obligations” for its exit from Russia. The German financial institution said Wednesday it has scaled back its presence in Russia since 2014, and its gross loan exposure[https://www.db.com/news/detail/20220309-deutsche-bank-reports-very-limited-russia-exposure?language_id=1] to the country stood at around $1.5 billion, with net exposure reduced to $650 million after accounting for guarantees and collateral.

Deutsche Bank initially declined to leave the Russian market after the invasion of Ukraine, with CFO James von Moltke telling CNBC[https://www.cnbc.com/2022/03/10/deutsche-bank-says-not-practical-to-exit-russia-business.html] on Thursday the bank has a duty to continue serving its clients in Russia and leaving the country wouldn’t be “practical.” The decision drew criticism from Bill Browder, a former investor in Russia and staunch regime critic[https://www.wsj.com/articles/financier-bill-browder-plays-the-long-game-to-expose-russian-money-laundering-11561647331] who told Reuters[https://www.reuters.com/business/deutsche-bank-ceo-gets-20-pay-rise-2021-2022-03-11/] earlier Friday the move was “completely at odds with the international business community.”

JPMorgan Chase and Goldman Sachs announced earlier this week they will wind down their presence in Russia, joining a growing list of Western companies that have sought to exit the Russian market, likely due to a combination of public pressure and sanctions. U.S. Sanctions Billionaire Viktor Vekselberg And Russian Lawmakers

The Treasury Department announced[https://home.treasury.gov/news/press-releases/jy0650] a new round of sanctions Friday targeting Russian elites, including billionaire Viktor Vekselberg[https://www.forbes.com/profile/viktor-vekselberg/?sh=2dec594f30b1].

The U.S. sanctioned three members of Kremlin spokesman Dmitry Peskov's immediate family, along with the management board of VTB Bank, Russia's second-largest financial institution. Twelve members of the Russian State Duma who signed a letter urging Russian President Vladimir Putin to recognize two pro-Kremlin breakaway states in eastern Ukraine as independent were also sanctioned.

All of those sanctioned Friday will be blocked from accessing any property they may own in the U.S., among related penalties.

As part of the actions against Vekselberg, whose fortune Forbes estimates at $6 billion, Treasury blocked an Airbus A319 jet registered in Aruba and his Cook Islands-flagged yacht, which the government believes are worth a total of $180 million.

“Treasury continues to hold Russian officials to account for enabling Putin’s unjustified and unprovoked war,” Treasury Secretary Janet Yellen said in a statement. “Today’s actions also further isolates the severely damaged Russian economy by prohibiting trade in products that are key to the economic and financial interests of all Russian elites.”

March 11, 2022 6:18 PM EST

Russia Struggles With Improper Planning Two Weeks Into Invasion, U.S. Says; No Signs Belarus Joining Fight

With Russian forces continuing to struggle to meet their objectives in Ukraine two weeks after their invasion began, a senior U.S. defense official told reporters Friday that Russia did not properly plan supplies and resources for the scale of the operation.

Here are additional updates on the situation from the senior defense official:

* Russian forces have not moved closer to Kyiv since Thursday, though rear elements have been moved closer to the front of the advance, and portions of a 40-mile-long military convoy[https://www.cnn.com/2022/03/11/europe/kyiv-russian-convoy-intl/index.html] are moving off-road and into the tree line, which the U.S. believes is to better protect the vehicles from Ukrainian forces.

* After reports of air strikes in western Ukraine for the first time since the invasion began, the official said “long range” Russian missiles struck two airfields in Lutsk and Ivano-Frankivsk.

* Chernihiv, a city north of Kyiv, is now isolated by Russian troops, which are “right outside,” according to the official.

* Russia has fired nearly 810 missiles since the invasion began, with about half coming from inside Ukraine, and the other half coming from Russia and Belarus.

* Both Ukraine and Russia have about 90% of their combat power available, though the official noted Russia has an advantage in “sheer numbers.”

* The official said 80% of Ukraine’s fixed-wing aircraft are intact, but they are making limited numbers of sorties, with Ukraine effectively defending against Russian air attacks with surface-to-air missiles.

* The U.S. has seen no substantive indications that Belarusian troops have joined the Russian offensive.

* The U.S. is aware of recruiting efforts from Syria[https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/mar/11/putin-approves-russian-use-of-middle-east-fighters-against-ukraine], but has not seen evidence or indications of any arrivals from outside countries.

Stripping Russia of this status, which requires action from Congress, would allow the U.S. and other countries to impose higher tariffs on some Russian imports.

House Speaker Nancy Pelosi (D-Calif.) told[https://twitter.com/mkraju/status/1502313929069973504?s=20&t=J60PDqI4wRWfAEw-QVkn9g] reporters the House will move to revoke Russia’s trade status next week, adding she hopes it will receive “a strong, bipartisan vote.” Canada was the first major U.S. ally to strip[https://www.cbc.ca/player/play/2008998979598] Russia of most-favored nation status last week.

The U.S. is also banning imports of Russian alcohol, seafood and diamonds, Biden said. March 11, 2022 12:00 PM EST

UN Has ‘Credible Reports’ Of Russian Forces Using Cluster Bombs In Ukraine

The United Nations human rights office said[https://www.ohchr.org/EN/NewsEvents/Pages/DisplayNews.aspx?NewsID=28262&LangID=E] on Friday it has received “credible reports” of Russian forces deploying cluster munitions in populated areas in Ukraine.

Cluster munitions are a class of weapon that break apart in the air and scatter a large number of smaller weapons across a wide area. While not prohibited—neither Russia nor Ukraine are party to the treaty banning them—their use in civilian areas can constitute a war crime. Human rights[https://www.hrw.org/news/2022/03/04/ukraine-cluster-munitions-launched-kharkiv-neighborhoods] groups[https://www.amnesty.org/en/latest/news/2022/02/ukraine-cluster-munitions-kill-child-and-two-other-civilians-taking-shelter-at-a-preschool/], watchdogs and journalists[https://www.bellingcat.com/news/rest-of-world/2022/03/11/these-are-the-cluster-munitions-documented-by-ukrainian-civilians/] have repeatedly alleged[https://www.telegraph.co.uk/world-news/2022/03/10/what-cluster-bombs-ukraine-war-how-much-damage/] their indiscriminate use by Russian forces since the start of the war.

"Due to their wide area effects, the use of cluster munitions in populated areas is incompatible with the international humanitarian law principles governing the conduct of hostilities," UN spokesperson Liz Throssell said. Targeting civilians, area bombardment and “other forms of indiscriminate attacks” are banned under international law and “may amount to war crimes,” Throssell said.

Throssell told Forbes the organization is “in the process of corroborating” the reports.

Biden, EU And G7 Will Reportedly Call For Russia To Have Trading Privileges Removed

President Biden, the EU and G7 nations will reportedly call for Russia to be removed from a key system Friday that prevents trading partners from discriminating against one another, Reuters[https://www.reuters.com/markets/europe/us-steps-up-pressure-russia-over-ukraine-invasion-2022-03-11/], Bloomberg[https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2022-03-11/biden-set-to-call-for-end-of-russia-s-preferred-trade-status] and the Associated Press[https://apnews.com/] report.

Under the “ most favored nation[https://www.wto.org/english/thewto_e/whatis_e/tif_e/fact2_e.htm] ” principle set up by the World Trade Organization, countries are prevented from giving special privileges to one country over others, ensuring equal treatment between members.

A removal of that status would further punish Russia economically by allowing countries to slap higher tariffs on Russian goods. It follows Western nations and allies continuing to sanction[https://www.forbes.com/sites/siladityaray/2022/03/10/uk-sanctions-roman-abramovich-and-six-other-russian-oligarchs-freezes-assets-including-chelsea-fc/?sh=5b9ba0f24bbd] Russian oligarchs and financial institutions, the U.S. banning the import[https://www.forbes.com/sites/sergeiklebnikov/2022/03/08/heres-how-bidens-historic-ban-on-russian-oil-will-hit-the-economy/?sh=45a5271659d3] of Russian gas and oil, and multinational firms[https://www.forbes.com/sites/marisadellatto/2022/03/10/goldman-sachs-amazon-3m-here-are-the-companies-cutting-ties-with-russia-over-ukraine-invasion/?sh=3e3ade3167de] either pausing business in Russia or completely pulling out over its invasion of Ukraine.

Biden Will Reportedly Push To End Normal Trade Relations With Russia

President Joe Biden will announce a plan Friday to revoke permanent normal trade relations with Russia, making it easier to impose additional tariffs on Russian goods, several[https://apnews.com/article/russia-ukraine-biden-business-european-union-europe-d86084ccec8d2ef338ed5a0432c34a1b] news[https://www.nytimes.com/live/2022/03/10/world/ukraine-russia-war?name=styln-russia-ukraine®ion=TOP_BANNER&block=storyline_menu_recirc&action=click&pgtype=LegacyCollection&variant=1_BlueLink&is_new=false] outlets[https://www.cnn.com/2022/03/10/politics/russia-trade-status-us-g7-eu-biden/index.html] reported[https://www.wsj.com/articles/biden-to-call-for-revoking-normal-trade-relations-with-russia-11646971099], citing unnamed sources.

The measure will require action from Congress, but both Democratic and Republican lawmakers have backed[https://gop-waysandmeans.house.gov/brady-smith-introduce-bipartisan-bicameral-house-bill-responding-to-russias-invasion-of-ukraine/] the[https://gop-waysandmeans.house.gov/brady-smith-introduce-bipartisan-bicameral-house-bill-responding-to-russias-invasion-of-ukraine/] idea[https://www.finance.senate.gov/chairmans-news/wyden-introduces-no-most-favored-nation-trading-with-russia-act-in-response-to-putins-unprovoked-invasion-of-ukraine] in recent days.

The European Union and the G7—a group of wealthy countries that includes the U.S.—are reportedly expected to unveil similar moves.

Since Russia invaded Ukraine two weeks ago, the United States and its allies have unveiled several rounds of penalties aimed at choking Russia’s economy. The Biden Administration has sanctioned Russia’s central bank[https://home.treasury.gov/news/press-releases/jy0612] and the country’s two largest banks[https://www.forbes.com/sites/joewalsh/2022/02/24/biden-announces-new-sanctions-on-russian-banks-and-tech-imports-following-ukraine-invasion/], banned Russian oil and gas imports[https://www.forbes.com/sites/sergeiklebnikov/2022/03/08/heres-how-bidens-historic-ban-on-russian-oil-will-hit-the-economy/?sh=197941a9659d] and imposed sanctions on several[https://www.forbes.com/sites/annakaplan/2022/03/03/us-sanctions-7-russian-oligarchs-and-kremlin-press-secretary-peskov/?sh=695a4a716b6e] Russian oligarchs and politicians.

Forbes has reached out to the White House for comment.

— Joe Walsh

March 11, 2022 3:38 AM EST

Senate Passes $13.6 Billion In Aid For Ukraine

The U.S. Senate passed a $1.5 trillion spending package that includes $13.6 billion in emergency aid for Ukraine late Thursday. The bill passed the House on Wednesday and is now headed to the desk of President Joe Biden, who is expected to sign it.

The bill will fund normal government operations until late September, staving off a government shutdown set to begin Friday. The Ukraine-related portion includes[https://www.cnn.com/2022/03/09/politics/ukraine-aid-spending-bill-congress/index.html] military and economic aid[https://appropriations.house.gov/sites/democrats.appropriations.house.gov/files/Ukraine%20Fact%20Sheet.pdf] for Ukraine as it fights back against Russian troops, as well as assistance to neighboring countries that have accepted millions of refugees and additional funding for the Pentagon, which has deployed extra troops to nearby NATO member states.

The bill passed the Senate 68-31, with 18 Republicans joining every Democrat to vote in favor, and it passed the House by similar bipartisan margins.

Read more here[https://www.forbes.com/sites/jonathanponciano/2022/03/10/senate-passes-15-trillion-spending-bill-with-14-billion-for-ukraine-aid-heres-whats-in-it/?sh=5256257cb713].

March 11, 2022 3:02 AM EST

Biden Administration Warns Of ‘Economic Pain’ If Russia Seizes Assets From Companies That Leave

White House Press Secretary Jen Psaki castigated Russian officials on Thursday for suggesting they may try to seize the assets of multinational corporations that have abandoned the Russian market in recent weeks, warning the move could further isolate the country’s economy.

Russian President Vladimir Putin said Thursday this government will seek “legal solutions” to the corporate exodus, including imposing “external management” and then “transfer[ring] these enterprises to those who want to work,” according to CNN[https://www.cnn.com/2022/03/10/business/russia-nationalization-western-companies/index.html] and the Financial Times[https://www.ft.com/content/0284a4e6-c005-46e7-992a-9e1bdd5ad0d5]. Under one proposal[https://www.telegraph.co.uk/business/2022/03/10/ftse-100-index-markets-live-news-oil-price-sanctions/] reportedly[https://www.ft.com/content/0284a4e6-c005-46e7-992a-9e1bdd5ad0d5] floated[https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/mar/10/russia-plans-to-seize-assets-of-western-companies-exiting-country] by the economic ministry, departing firms whose foreign ownership exceeds 25% could be temporarily[https://www.washingtonpost.com/business/2022/03/10/russia-nationalize-foreign-business-ukraine/] placed under local management.

In a series of tweets[https://twitter.com/PressSec/status/1502099524147453953?s=20&t=Rdxd80dw5KGRikyWVsttyA] Thursday evening, Psaki said any move to seize assets “will ultimately result in even more economic pain for Russia” by signaling to other foreign enterprises that the country “is not a safe place to invest and do business.” Russia could also face stiff legal blowback, Psaki warned.

Since Russia invaded Ukraine, dozens of Western companies[https://www.forbes.com/sites/marisadellatto/2022/03/10/goldman-sachs-amazon-3m-here-are-the-companies-cutting-ties-with-russia-over-ukraine-invasion/?sh=ef5347767dea] like JPMorgan Chase, Goldman Sachs and BP have announced plans to suspend business in Russia or exit joint ventures in the country. The moves come amid public outcry[https://www.forbes.com/sites/lisakim/2022/03/10/from-mars-to-uniqlo-these-major-companies-are-continuing-to-do-business-in-russia/?sh=642796b93983] and hefty sanctions that make it difficult to do business[https://www.npr.org/sections/money/2022/03/08/1084280072/how-sanctions-are-pinning-down-the-russian-economy] in Russia. Psaki said any voluntary moves to leave the Russian market are “ultimately up to companies,” but the Biden Administration “welcome[s] the decisions.”

March 11, 2022 1:47 AM EST

Large Russian Convoy Disperses Outside Kyiv

A large convoy of Russian military vehicles last seen northwest of Ukraine’s capital of Kyiv has “largely dispersed” into surrounding areas, according to satellite images taken by Maxar Technologies Thursday morning.

The convoy was last spotted near Antonov airport, which is about 17 miles northwest of central Kyiv. Its vehicles have been redeployed: Some military vehicles have moved through towns near the airport—an area that has seen heavy fighting in recent days—and some units entered forests a few miles north, according to Maxar.

Maxar spotted a 40-mile-long Russian convoy that ended at Antonov airport last week. The convoy, which included armored vehicles[https://www.bbc.com/news/world-europe-60596629] and artillery, was stalled for days due to heavy Ukrainian resistance and logistical issues[https://www.bbc.com/news/world-europe-60596629], a senior U.S. defense official told reporters last week. Maxar did not specify whether the stalled 40-mile convoy is the same column that appeared to be dispersed in Thursday’s images.

Russia’s advance toward Kyiv has been slower than expected, amid intense fighting from Ukrainian forces. Some Russian troops are now just over nine miles northwest from Kyiv, a senior U.S. defense official said Thursday.

Images taken by Maxar on Thursday also showed property damage near Kyiv and in the northern city of Chernihiv, which is now surrounded by Russian forces[https://www.nytimes.com/2022/03/10/world/europe/chernihiv-russia-troops.html], according to local and U.S. officials.

Residential buildings are heavily damaged in Borodyanka, about 30 miles northwest of Kyiv, in satellite images taken Thursday.Satellite image ©2022 Maxar Technologies[https://specials-images.forbesimg.com/imageserve/622aacccfbf6e9b70fe6e781/960x0.jpg?fit=scale]

Cars line up near a destroyed bridge, amid evacuation efforts in the Kyiv suburb of Irpin.Satellite image ©2022 Maxar Technologies[https://specials-images.forbesimg.com/imageserve/622aad73cf993507646f32c9/960x0.jpg?fit=scale]

A shopping center in the northern city of Chernihiv was heavily damaged as Russian troops encircled the city.Satellite image ©2022 Maxar Technologies[https://specials-images.forbesimg.com/imageserve/622aadb14f4a696b6c7a7528/960x0.jpg?fit=scale]

March 11, 2022 12:34 AM EST

Senators Urge Biden To Reconsider Nixing Transfer Of Polish Jets To Ukraine

The vast majority of the Republican Senate caucus urged the Biden administration Thursday to reverse its opposition to supplying Ukraine with Soviet-era warplanes from Poland—a proposition the Pentagon said[https://www.forbes.com/sites/joewalsh/2022/03/09/pentagon-opposes-sending-mig-29-fighter-jets-to-ukraine/?sh=3abdf8284483] would’ve been too “high risk” without providing much aid to Ukrainian forces.

In a letter[https://www.ernst.senate.gov/public/_cache/files/27f1a32f-aa03-460b-a6da-05fd93b251a0/EEAC805CD285FCBD6B3AE1C0ED6869A0.2022-03-10-final-ernst-ukraine-letter-to-biden.pdf] addressed to the president, 42 Republican senators called on the administration to “work with Poland and our NATO allies to expedite the transfer of urgently-needed airpower, air defense systems, and other combat and support capabilities,” pointing to Russia’s attacks on Ukrainian civilians and commending the Biden Administration for the aid it has sent to Ukraine.

Pentagon spokesperson John Kirby said Wednesday the U.S. would not take up Poland on its public offer to fly dozens of its MiG-29 fighters to a German air base used by U.S. forces, from which it proposed the U.S. could transport them to Ukraine, stating that supplying the planes to Ukraine was a decision for the Polish government. Kirby also said the Pentagon did not think it made sense to give the planes to Ukraine at all, stating it“is not likely to significantly change the effectiveness of the Ukrainian air force relative to Russian capabilities,” and it “may be mistaken as escalatory” by Russia.

Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky requested[https://www.nytimes.com/2022/03/05/us/politics/zelensky-congress-no-fly-zone.html] warplanes in a Zoom meeting with members of the U.S. Congress last weekend, in addition to the imposition of a no-fly zone—an ask the Biden Administration has said[https://www.state.gov/secretary-antony-j-blinken-on-nbc-meet-the-press-with-chuck-todd/] is a non-starter as it would put the U.S. in direct conflict with Russia.

The Pentagon is considering facilitating the transfer to Ukraine of mid- and high-altitude Russian-made anti-aircraft systems[https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-allies-look-at-sending-soviet-era-air-defense-systems-to-ukraine-11646933998] that would give the Ukrainians a powerful complement to the portable Stinger anti-aircraft missiles that the U.S. has provided.

March 10, 2022 11:28 PM EST

Ukraine Claims Civilian Deaths Outnumber Troop Deaths—And About 280 Schools Were Damaged

Ukrainian Defense Minister Oleksiy Reznikov said Thursday Russian forces have killed more Ukrainian civilians than military personnel since they first invaded two weeks ago. The claim has not been independently verified, and Reznikov didn’t list the number of military or civilian deaths in his Thursday evening Facebook post[https://www.facebook.com/100063790800238/videos/501003601588184/?__so__=watchlist&__rv__=video_home_www_playlist_video_listhttps%3A%2F%2Ft.me%2FSerhiyShkarlet%2F717].

“The Kremlin is bombing schools and hospitals, including maternity hospitals. Moscow does not protect anyone. It destroys,” Reznikov said of the death tolls. Ukrainian officials said at least 17 people were wounded Wednesday after airstrikes and shelling destroyed a maternity hospital[https://www.forbes.com/sites/siladityaray/2022/03/10/photos-russian-airstrikes-demolish-maternity-hospital-and-houses-in-mariupol/?sh=42bd7b644eb5] and other civilian buildings in the besieged port city of Mariupol.

The Ukrainian Ministry of Education and Science also said[https://www.facebook.com/UAMON/posts/295194329387053?__cft__[0]=AZUaIRSDXk6pR7utRJOVCDUS-CuAjd3hLTUmBeBhimv8gKBlg86WxxV4O3ZDEIuG_yJJY5SG9RXXrMa-jaWcusPeNHQ1nb9A5neRUkhvidHeksVoav2k7WtPtI3aEOk8ASn3Bi9CnLcsjERVksvrIUJy&__tn__=%2CO%2CP-R] in a Thursday Facebook post more than 280 educational institutions have been damaged or destroyed by bombing and shelling. The ministry linked a website[https://saveschools.in.ua/] showing nine schools had been destroyed and 277 had been damaged, citing news outlets and videos of damaged buildings.

Since the war began, both Russian and Ukrainian officials have made claims about civilian and military fatality counts that haven’t been independently verified by third parties.

The United Nations has verified[https://www.ohchr.org/EN/NewsEvents/Pages/DisplayNews.aspx?NewsID=28233&LangID=E] 549 civilian deaths during Russia’s invasion, including 41 children, though it said Thursday the actual death toll could be “considerably higher.” The UN pointed to “heavy artillery and multi-launch rocket systems, and missile and air strikes” as reasons for many of the deaths. Nearly 1,000 more civilians have been injured, the UN said.

March 10, 2022 9:55 PM EST

Russian Forces Move 3 Miles Closer To Kyiv, U.S. Says

Some Russian units have moved about three miles closer to Kyiv in the last 24 hours, a senior U.S. defense official said Thursday, as Russian troops approach the capital city from multiple angles and appear to be aiming to encircle it—but their advance is facing heavy resistance.

Some of the Russian forces that moved forward—an advance the senior defense official cast as relatively modest—could be less than 10 miles northwest of central Kyiv, and are largely “in the vicinity of” an airport in the town of Hostomel. Irpin and other towns near the airport have faced[https://www.nytimes.com/2022/03/09/world/europe/ukraine-family-perebyinis-kyiv.html#:~:text=They%20didn't%20make%20it,trying%20to%20evacuate%20to%20Kyiv.] heavy fighting[https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/2022/03/08/ukraine-russia-irpin-war/] in recent days, and the U.K. Ministry of Defense accused Russia[https://twitter.com/DefenceHQ/status/1500981170280050688?ref_src=twsrc%5Egoogle%7Ctwcamp%5Eserp%7Ctwgr%5Etweet] of intentionally targeting evacuation routes in the area earlier this week, leading to multiple civilian deaths[https://www.nytimes.com/2022/03/06/world/europe/ukraine-irpin-civilian-death.html] in Irpin.

Russia is also approaching Kyiv from the east, along two parallel lines that are as close as 25 miles from the capital, according to the U.S. defense official. And Russian forces have “isolated” Chernihiv, a city of almost 300,000 people that lies about 80 miles northeast of Kyiv, though that contingent hasn’t moved past Chernihiv.

Russia has aimed to encircle Ukrainian cities like Chernihiv in the north and Mariupol in the south, but surrounding Kyiv—a sprawling city of around 3 million—could be a more difficult task, especially as Ukrainian forces continue to put up stiff resistance. The senior U.S. defense official said it’s tough to predict how long it will take Russia to encircle Kyiv, but an unnamed American official told NBC News on Thursday[https://www.nbcnews.com/news/world/live-blog/ukraine-russia-war-live-updates-top-diplomats-meet-talks-kyiv-n1291419] it could take one or two weeks, and seizing control of Kyiv would likely take four weeks or more.

“Kyiv is being well-defended, and the Ukrainians are putting a lot of energy … into protecting their capital city,” the senior defense official told reporters Thursday.

U.S. Ambassador to the United Nations Linda Thomas-Greenfield told BBC Newshour Thursday that Russia is committing "war crimes" in Ukraine, becoming the highest ranking American official to accuse Russia of doing so.

The White House has criticized Russian attacks as "atrocities" but has been careful about alleging war crimes, with Press Secretary Jen Psaki saying[https://twitter.com/kelliemeyernews/status/1501986278752980995?s=21] Thursday the Biden Administration was waiting for a "legal assessment" before making any war crimes claim.

March 10, 2022 9:26 PM EST

European Leaders Rule Out Ukraine’s Hopes Of ‘Fast-Tracking’ Its EU Membership

Western European leaders said at a European Union summit Thursday they won’t grant Ukraine’s request to let the country swiftly join the EU[https://www.forbes.com/sites/alisondurkee/2022/02/28/will-ukraine-join-the-eu-after-zelensky-signs-membership-application-heres-what-could-come-next/?sh=5410f4d368c7] by creating a “fast-track” procedure, instead requiring it to go through the normal years-long membership process. “Can we open today a procedure of accession with a country at war? I don’t think so,” French President Emmanuel Macron said, according to the Associated Press[https://apnews.com/article/russia-ukraine-putin-business-france-europe-bd12fb7b215140a200d1c87a6fce01a3], while Dutch Prime Minister Mark Rutte stressed[https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/mar/10/western-europe-leaders-rebuff-ukraine-fast-track-eu-membership-appeal] “there is no fast-track procedure to become a member of the EU.”

Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky signed Ukraine’s application to join the EU on February 28 after Russia’s invasion and called for Ukraine’s “immediate accession under a new special procedure.” Speeding up the process had been viewed as a long shot, but had gained steam with a number of leaders in Eastern European countries, including the Czech Republic, Poland, Slovakia and Slovenia. EU leadership has been supportive of Ukraine eventually joining the bloc, but without a quicker procedure the process will likely stretch on for years; Croatia, the last country admitted, joined[https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2022-02-28/ukraine-wants-eu-membership-but-accession-usually-takes-years?sref=LfZwdw4Z] the EU in 2013 after a 10-year process.

Russia’s invasion of Ukraine has killed 549 civilians, including 41 children, according[https://www.ohchr.org/EN/NewsEvents/Pages/DisplayNews.aspx?NewsID=28233&LangID=E] to the United Nations, though the organization noted it believes the actual figures are “considerably higher.” The UN’s Office of the High Commissioner for Human Rights said an additional 957 people, 52 of whom are children, have been injured since the invasion began two weeks ago.

Russia Has No Ceasefire Plans, But Ukraine Will Not Surrender: Ukrainian Foreign Minister

Thursday’s meeting between Ukrainian foreign minister Dmytro Kuleba and Russian Foreign Minister Sergei Lavrov in Turkey ended with no progress made on a possible ceasefire.

In a press conference following the meeting, Kuleba said Russia had no plans to establish a ceasefire and was instead seeking a surrender from Ukraine. “This is not what they're going to get. Ukraine is strong, Ukraine is fighting.”

Kuleba said that Ukraine has ensured the failure of Russia’s initial invasion plans and was now seeking a diplomatic resolution to the conflict. “But we will not surrender.” The Ukrainian minister added he spent most the time during the meeting trying to address humanitarian issues, seeking a humanitarian corridor in the port city of Mariupol where a maternity hospital was hit by Russian strikes on Wednesday.

According to Kuleba, Lavrov told him he did not have the authority to agree this measure, but would take the proposal back to the Kremlin. The Ukrainian minister then added: “Lavrov has a different impression of what foreign ministers do in a crisis.”

House Passes Bipartisan Bill Banning Russian Energy Imports

The House on Wednesday passed a bipartisan bill that will ban Russian energy imports—after its final passage in the Senate—building on an executive order signed by President Joe Biden Tuesday.

The Suspending Energy Imports from Russia passed 414 to 17, with 194 Republicans joining 220 Democrats in support, setting up a final vote in the Senate as soon as Thursday.

The 17-page bill will suspend the import of Russian oil and energy products into the U.S. beginning 45 days after the legislation is enacted but give Biden the authority to waive the ban for any products necessary for the "national interest of the United States.”

Between 5,000 and 6,000 Russian troops have been killed during the first two weeks of Russia’s invasion of Ukraine, a U.S. official told various news outlets. That number is significantly higher than the Pentagon’s previous estimate of between 2,000 to 4,000 deaths shared[https://www.deccanherald.com/international/world-news-politics/pentagon-estimates-2000-to-4000-russian-soldiers-killed-in-ukraine-1089394.html] earlier this week.

While there are no estimates from the number of wounded Russian soldiers, CBS notes that they are generally around three times as many killed, putting that estimate between 15,000 to 18,000.

March 10, 2022 2:17 AM EST

IMF Approves $1.4 Billion Disbursement To Ukraine

The International Monetary Fund has approved $1.4 billion in emergency financing for Ukraine, the international lender announced[https://www.imf.org/en/News/Articles/2022/03/09/pr2269-ukriane-imf-executive-board-approves-usd-billion-in-emergency-financing-support-to-ukraine] Wednesday.

“The war in Ukraine is resulting in tragic loss of life and human suffering,” the IMF said in its release[https://www.imf.org/en/News/Articles/2022/03/09/pr2269-ukriane-imf-executive-board-approves-usd-billion-in-emergency-financing-support-to-ukraine]. “While the outlook is subject to extraordinary uncertainty, the economic consequences are already very serious.”

The IMF said it will disburse the loan using its Rapid Financing Instrument, an emergency assistance policy designed to quickly provide funds during a conflict or natural disaster.

The IMF’s emergency funding follows a $723 million support package for Ukraine approved[https://www.worldbank.org/en/news/press-release/2022/03/07/world-bank-mobilizes-an-emergency-financing-package-of-over-700-million-for-ukraine] Monday by the World Bank. The bank said another $3 billion package would be sent “in the coming months” for Ukraine and its neighbors, which are receiving millions of refugees.

U.S. lawmakers said it aims to pass a $13.6 billion package that includes military and humanitarian aid for Ukraine, unnamed members of Congress and aides told[https://www.nytimes.com/live/2022/03/08/world/ukraine-russia-war/lawmakers-close-in-on-agreement-to-provide-nearly-14-billion-in-military-and-humanitarian-aid-for-ukraine] the New York Times Tuesday. The aid is expected to be passed in a larger government spending package that Congress must approve by Friday in order to avoid a government shutdown, following last month’s approval of a short-term spending bill.

‘He Sees That We Are Strong’: Zelensky Thinks Putin Will Eventually End Hostilities In Ukraine

Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky told[https://www.vice.com/en/article/xgdm37/volodymyr-zelenskyy-interview-ukraine-russia] Vice News on Wednesday he believes Russian President Vladimir Putin will eventually “stop the war” and “begin to speak,” as Ukrainian forces continue to push back against invading Russian troops.

“I think he sees that we are strong,” Zelensky said in an in-person interview with Vice. “He will [cease fighting]. We need some time.”

Zelensky added that he does not trust Putin: “I only trust my family,” he said.

Russian Foreign Minister Sergey Lavrov and Ukrainian Foreign Minister Dmytro Kuleba are expected to meet[https://twitter.com/MevlutCavusoglu/status/1500798954379919362?ref_src=twsrc%5Egoogle%7Ctwcamp%5Eserp%7Ctwgr%5Etweet] in Turkey on Thursday. Russia has bombarded Ukrainian cities in recent weeks, but its ground forces have struggled to make extensive progress in the country’s north. Western officials say the invasion’s slower-than-expected pace is partly due to stiff resistance from Ukrainians.

Russia ‘Likely’ Deploying Private Mercenaries In Ukraine, U.K. Says

The British Ministry of Defense said[https://twitter.com/defencehq/status/1501701763921059845?s=21] in an intelligence update Wednesday Russia is “likely deploying” private military companies as part of its invasion of Ukraine.

Mercenaries were reportedly deployed in Ukraine weeks before Russia began its invasion, unnamed security officials told Reuters[https://www.reuters.com/world/europe/exclusive-russian-mercenaries-with-spy-links-increasing-presence-ukraine-western-2022-02-14/] last month. The U.S. had warned of Russia using private contractors to stage “false flag” operations in Ukraine to justify its eventual invasion.

Mercenaries from the Wagner Group—a secretive Russian contracting organization—appeared to enter regions of eastern Ukraine controlled by pro-Russian separatists, the New York Times[https://www.nytimes.com/2022/02/23/world/europe/russian-ukraine-mercenaries.html] reported hours before the invasion started two weeks ago. A senior U.S. defense official told reporters last week there are indications the Wagner Group is present in Ukraine, but it’s unclear how many of their staff are participating in the war.

Private military contractors such as the Wagner Group have been accused of committing human rights violations in several countries, including the Central African Republic and Syria[https://www.france24.com/en/europe/20211213-eu-imposes-sanctions-on-russian-mercenary-group-wagner-over-human-rights-abuses]. Last year, the United Nations raised concerns[https://www.ohchr.org/EN/NewsEvents/Pages/DisplayNews.aspx?NewsID=27717&LangID=E] the group—operating as security and military personnel in the Central African Republic—committed crimes such as arbitrary detention, torture, disappearances and summary execution, along with reports of rape and sexual violence. Russia has denied ties to the Wagner Group[https://www.bbc.com/news/world-africa-58009514].

White House Press Secretary Jen Psaki tweeted Wednesday the world should "be on the lookout" for Russia possibly using chemical or biological weapons in Ukraine, after the Kremlin accused the U.S. of backing a bioweapons program for the Ukrainian government.

Click to access link.[https://embedly.forbes.com/widgets/media.html?type=text%2Fhtml&key=3ce26dc7e3454db5820ba084d28b4935&schema=twitter&url=https%3A//twitter.com/presssec/status/1501676240180322311&image=https%3A//i.embed.ly/1/image%3Furl%3Dhttps%253A%252F%252Fabs.twimg.com%252Ferrors%252Flogo46x38.png%26key%3D3ce26dc7e3454db5820ba084d28b4935]

Russian foreign ministry spokesperson Maria Zakharova claimed without offering any proof Wednesday that Russia had amassed evidence of a U.S.-backed chemical weapons program in Ukraine, which Psaki called "preposterous."

Psaki said Russia might be setting the stage for a false flag operation to use its own chemical weapons in Ukraine, noting Russia has "long maintained a biological weapons program in violation of international law" and backs the regime of Syrian President Bashar al-Assad, which has used chemical weapons in the Syrian civil war.

"Russia has a track record of accusing the West of the very violations that Russia itself is perpetrating," Psaki tweeted. "This is all an obvious ploy by Russia to try to try to justify its further premeditated, unprovoked, and unjustified attack on Ukraine."

Pentagon Press Secretary John Kirby said Wednesday there currently isn’t any intelligence indicating that Russia has brought chemical or biological weapons into Ukraine, but he warned “it is … a piece of the Russian playbook to blame others for that which you are about to do.”

Russian President Vladimir Putin justified the invasion of Ukraine by falsely claiming ethnic Russians were facing a genocide in the Donbas region and inconceivably accusing Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky, who is Jewish, of heading a neo-Nazi government.

Russia May Be Dropping ‘Dumb Bombs’ In Ukraine As Patriot Missile Batteries Reach Poland, U.S. Says

The U.S. has seen indications Russia is using “dumb bombs,” or unguided munitions that have limited ability to hit precise targets, a senior U.S. defense official told reporters Wednesday. Military experts told[https://www.washingtonpost.com/national-security/2022/03/01/ukraine-invasion-russia-missiles-siege/] the Washington Post last week Russia may be experiencing a shortage of specific weapons, including guided munitions, as Russian forces appeared to begin their attack in Ukraine with guided missiles, but began firing weapons that can be difficult to aim precisely days after the invasion began.

The senior U.S. defense official also confirmed the two Patriot missile batteries the Pentagon said it would send to Poland on Tuesday have been delivered, and that “they are manned.” The U.S. European Command said in a statement[https://twitter.com/DanLamothe/status/1501390081776664580?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw] the repositioning of the Patriot batteries would “proactively” counter “any potential threat” to the U.S. and its allies in NATO territory.

Polish officials signed[https://www.reuters.com/article/us-raytheon-poland-patriot/poland-signs-4-75-billion-deal-for-u-s-patriot-missile-system-facing-russia-idUSKBN1H417S] an agreement in 2018 to receive the batteries, which were due to be delivered by the end of this year. Poland also hosts one of the two Aegis Ashore[https://www.defense.gov/News/News-Stories/Article/Article/2849023/aegis-ashore-in-poland-on-target-for-2022/#:~:text=The%20Aegis%20Ashore%20%22Deckhouse%22%20command,by%20the%20end%20of%202022.] missile defenses in Europe, which Russian officials have expressed concerns[https://foreignpolicy.com/2022/01/12/putin-russia-us-missile-defense-nato-ukraine/] about, fearing the U.S. could use the defensive batteries to fire offensive attacks. U.S. officials offered to allow Moscow to inspect the facility last month in an effort to ease tensions, USNI reports[https://news.usni.org/2022/02/02/u-s-offered-russians-aegis-ashore-inspections-to-ease-ukraine-tensions-more-american-troops-headed-to-europe].

Here are additional updates from the 14th day of Russia’s invasion, according to the senior defense official:

* The official said the U.S. sees “increasing damage to civilian infrastructure and civilian casualties” in Ukraine.

* Russian forces appear to be “just outside of the city” in Kharkiv, and have moved about 12 miles closer since Tuesday, though there is still “heavy fighting.”

* In Mykolaiv, Russian troops have advanced from the north and are about 9 miles outside of the city.

* There has been “no real ground progress to speak to” in Kyiv, Chernihiv and Mariupol.

* The U.S. is very close to delivering the $350 million aid package approved[https://www.reuters.com/world/biden-approves-350-million-military-aid-ukraine-2022-02-26/] by President Joe Biden at the end of February, and the official expects it will be completely closed out “in the coming days.”

* Russia has launched more than 710 missiles since the invasion began, with the majority coming from Russia and inside Ukraine.

* The airspace remains contested over Ukraine, and Russia has not reached air superiority.

March 9, 2022 5:14 PM EST

Retired Military Leaders Call For Supplying Ukraine With Air Defense Systems

A group of retired senior military leaders from the United States and Europe have penned a letter[https://s3.documentcloud.org/documents/21399038/an-open-letter-to-the-leaders-of-democratic-governments-of-the-world.pdf] obtained by CNN that calls for transferring Russian-made air defense systems to Ukraine to thwart Russia's continued bombardment of the country.

The military leaders proposed that countries with similar mid- and high-altitude air defense systems to Ukrainian ones that have been destroyed should transfer them to the besieged nation, saying the world has a "moral duty and humanitarian obligation" to limit the damage Russia is inflicting.

The retired generals and admirals framed the move as an alternative to creating a "no-fly zone" over Ukraine. NATO and U.S. officials have dismissed Ukraine’s entreaties to establish one, arguing that shooting down Russian planes could spark a full-blown world war.

"We cannot stand idly by and wish them well as Russia prosecutes an unrestricted campaign of destruction on the Ukrainian government, its infrastructure, and its people," the letter said.

Signatories include retired U.S. Air Force Gen. Phillip M. Breedlove, who served as supreme allied commander Europe; Sir Richard Shireff, a former U.K. general who served as deputy supreme allied commander Europe; and retired generals from Germany, Latvia, Lithuania and Estonia.

The International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA) said[https://twitter.com/iaeaorg/status/1501545852715905029?s=20&t=fUkqZJZHc1Rnr0Mvdaz09w] Wednesday morning a power outage at the Chernobyl nuclear power plant presents “no critical impact on safety” shortly after Ukraine’s government said a radioactive leak appeared imminent. Even without electricity, the IAEA said[https://twitter.com/iaeaorg/status/1501545852715905029?s=20&t=fUkqZJZHc1Rnr0Mvdaz09w] Chernobyl’s cooling mechanism are sufficient to effectively cool spent nuclear fuel and thus block the release of nuclear discharge.

Earlier Wednesday, Ukraine’s state security communications service reported[https://twitter.com/dsszzi/status/1501518949703426051?s=20&t=OMzdjJRlho9wSg6-sSZvRw] Russian forces damaged a power line to Chernobyl, claiming the plant will not be able to prevent the release of radioactive substances without electricity. March 9, 2022 12:47 PM EST

Power Outage At Chernobyl Threatens Radioactive Leak, Ukraine Warns

Radioactive materials could be released from Ukraine’s Chernobyl nuclear power plant after a power line to the station was cut, the Ukrainian government said[https://twitter.com/dsszzi/status/1501518949703426051?s=20&t=8zUgGC2fYiOXqYYRRxKLwA] Wednesday. The power plant relies on electricity to cool spent nuclear fuel, which can release radioactive substances if allowed to overheat. All personnel at Chernobyl will receive a “dangerous dose of radiation” if this happens, the government warned, and wind could carry a radioactive cloud to other parts of Ukraine, Belarus, Russia and across Europe.

Nuclear power plants typically have backup sources of power, though it is unclear whether these are operational after fighting around the plant.

The Chernobyl plant, the site of the world’s worst accidental nuclear disaster, is under Russian control and officials say they do not know what levels of radiation there are at the plant. On Tuesday, the UN’s atomic energy agency said it was no longer receiving monitoring data from Chernobyl.

March 9, 2022 2:17 AM EST

Fitch Downgrades Russia’s Credit Rating Amid Fears Of ‘Imminent’ Sovereign Default

Credit agency Fitch Ratings lowered[https://www.fitchratings.com/research/sovereigns/fitch-downgrades-russia-to-c-08-03-2022] Russia’s sovereign rating from a ‘B’ to a ‘C’ Tuesday, warning a default on its debt is “imminent” as Russia faces harsh sanctions and trade restrictions following its invasion of Ukraine.

Fitch and the other two major U.S. credit rating agencies—Moody’s Investors Service and Standard & Poors—downgraded Russia to junk status within a week of the invasion, and have further downgraded the country in recent days. Moody’s lowered[https://www.moodys.com/research/Moodys-downgrades-Russias-ratings-to-Ca-from-B3-the-outlook--PR_463675] its rating of Russia to a ‘Ca’ on Sunday, and S&P lowered[https://www.reuters.com/markets/deals/sp-drags-russias-rating-deeper-into-junk-territory-2022-03-03/] its rating to ‘CCC-minus’ last week.

In a press release Tuesday, Fitch cited a recent decision[https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2022-03-05/putin-seeks-to-avert-defaults-with-ruble-payments-to-creditors] by Russia to let the government and companies pay foreign creditors in rubles. The firm also warned that sanctions on Russia—including new limits on the country’s crucial oil export industry—“increase the probability of a policy response by Russia that includes at least selective non-payment of its sovereign debt obligations.”

U.S. Rejects Poland’s Offer To Hand Over MiG-29s To Aid Ukraine

The Pentagon on Tuesday rejected Poland’s offer to transfer dozens of aging Soviet-made MiG-29 fighter jets to the United States, which could then hand the planes off to Ukraine.

Earlier Tuesday, Poland proposed sending its MiG-29s—a model also flown by the Ukrainian air force—to the U.S.-run Ramstein Air Base in Germany “immediately and free of charge,” with the U.S. giving used fighter jets to Poland in return. The proposal came as NATO members looked for a way to give warplanes to Ukraine to aid in its fight against invading Russian troops.

But Pentagon Press Secretary John Kirby said hours later “we do not believe Poland’s proposal is a tenable one.” Kirby warned that sending planes from a base in Germany to contested Ukrainian airspace “raises serious concerns for the entire NATO alliance.”

Some U.S. officials told NBC News[https://www.nbcnews.com/politics/national-security/proposed-plan-sending-fighter-jets-ukraine-hits-logistical-snag-rcna19083] on Monday the prospect of transferring planes from Poland to Ukraine—with the U.S. military backfilling Poland’s supply—raised logistical challenges, and Russia may interpret the move as NATO’s entry into the war.

U.S. Threatens To Cut Off Chinese Companies If They Defy Russian Sanctions

The U.S. threatened to cut off supplies of vital semiconductors, microchips, and other American-made technology essential to Chinese companies if they continue to supply Russia with equipment, according to an interview in the New York Times[https://www.nytimes.com/2022/03/08/technology/chinese-companies-russia-semiconductors.html] Tuesday. Secretary of Commerce Gina Raimondo told the Times that the Chinese state-owned Semiconductor Manufacturing International Corporation (SMIC) and any other Chinese company that supplies equipment to Russia in defiance of U.S. sanctions could be cut off from U.S.-made supplies, which would "essentially shut" down those companies.

Under recently issued sanctions[https://www.whitehouse.gov/briefing-room/statements-releases/2022/02/24/fact-sheet-joined-by-allies-and-partners-the-united-states-imposes-devastating-costs-on-russia/] and export controls, companies like SMIC are not allowed to sell products that use American technology (even if the companies themselves are not American) to Russia. The White House said last week these restrictions were put in place to “choke off Russia’s import of technological goods critical to a diversified economy and Putin’s ability to project power.”

“We could essentially shut SMIC down because we prevent them from using our equipment and our software,” Raimondo told the Times.

Prior to Russia’s invasion, China had maintained close ties with its neighbor. Chinese foreign minister Wang Yi said at a press conference Monday, “The China-Russia relationship is valued for its independence,” adding his country aimed to keep ties “free from interference or discord sown by third parties,” according[https://www.cnbc.com/2022/03/07/china-upholds-its-relationship-with-russia-says-negotiations-needed-to-solve-ukraine-conflict.html] to CNBC.

March 8, 2022 6:35 PM EST

Russia Advances On Kyiv From New Direction — But ‘Stiff’ Ukrainian Resistance Slows Progress

A new column of Russian forces is advancing on Kyiv from the east as bombardment of the capital city continues, a senior U.S. defense official told reporters Tuesday, though Ukrainian resistance efforts are still slowing down Russian troops, particularly in northern Ukraine. The eastern advance toward Kyiv supports the U.S. assessment that Russia aims to encircle the city, according to the official.

Here are additional updates from the senior defense official:

* Russian troops are advancing on Kyiv from three sides, with a new force from the east about 37 miles outside of Kyiv.

* One contingent of Russian forces headed for Kyiv from the north is “stuck” in Chernihiv and “can’t get past it,” according to the official.

* The key port city of Mariupol is now isolated from Ukraine, and has been disconnected from the power grid since March 2, but “stiff” Ukrainian resistance has kept Russian forces from taking control of the city.

* Bombardment and shelling of Mykolaiv, a city near the Black Sea, has increased from yesterday, but Russian forces have not taken control of the city.

* Russian shelling damaged a major gas pipeline in eastern Ukraine—24 gas distribution centers were switched off and about 1 million consumers are without gas in the Donetsk and Zaporizhzhia regions.

* Nearly 100% of Russian forces assembled for the invasion have been sent into Ukraine, and about 95% of the combat power assembled is still “intact.”

* Nearly 670 missiles have been launched since the invasion began, with the majority coming from Russia and inside Ukraine.

* Most of Ukraine is under threat from Russian surface to air missiles, but Russia has not achieved air superiority, and Ukraine can still fly aircraft and conduct viable and effective missile defense.

* The U.S. cannot confirm that Russian Maj. Gen. Vitaly Gerasimov had been killed following a Ukrainian government claim that he died in a battle near Kharkiv.

March 8, 2022 4:34 PM EST

Biden Announces U.S. Ban On Russian Energy

President Joe Biden said[https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=riIbml4OyOY] Tuesday the U.S. will ban all imports of Russian energy, including oil and natural gas, in the latest step by the White House to cut off Russia economically for invading Ukraine. Biden said the move will target “the main artery of Russian economy” — energy products accounted for roughly 50% of Russia’s $489.8 billion in exports in 2021.

Russian crude accounted for 3% of U.S. oil imports in 2021, according to the trade group American Fuel & Petrochemical Manufacturers[https://www.afpm.org/newsroom/blog/oil-and-petroleum-imports-russia-explained].

Biden said the U.S. is working with European countries, which are heavy importers of Russian oil and gas, on a long-term strategy to reduce their reliance.

The U.K. will also stop importing Russian oil through a gradual phaseout over the course of 2022, Kwasi Kwarteng, the British secretary of state for business, energy and industrial strategy, announced[https://twitter.com/KwasiKwarteng/status/1501229850454937600?s=20&t=5zC55e0c6y8iyWUwdr6N5g] in a tweet.

London Metal Exchange Halts Trading Of Nickel As Price Soars To $100,000 A Tonne

The London Metal Exchange (LME) on Tuesday suspended[https://www.lme.com/api/sitecore/MemberNoticesSearchApi/Download?id=9be362b4-fa9e-41d5-b6a0-c4f1e7892e4c] trading of Nickel following an “unprecedented” 70% jump in its price to a record $100,000 a tonne.

Announcing the suspension, the exchange attributed the sharp increase to the “evolving situation in Russia and Ukraine,” forcing the LME to halt trading on “orderly market grounds.”

The massive surge in the price of Nickel—a key component in stainless steel and rechargeable batteries—comes as Washington mulls more severe sanctions against Russia, including a ban on all energy imports from the country. Russia is one of the world’s top producers[https://pubs.usgs.gov/periodicals/mcs2021/mcs2021-nickel.pdf] of Nickel which traders fear may be included in the import ban.

Humanitarian Corridor Created In Sumy, Ukraine Deputy Prime Minister Says

A humanitarian corridor was created in the city of Sumy, northeast Ukraine, Tuesday, allowing civilians safe passage from Russian attacks into other parts of the country, Deputy Prime Minister Iryna Vereshchuk said[https://www.instagram.com/p/Ca1ZFK5j8UN/].

Russia and Ukraine agreed to the move, Reuters reports[https://www.reuters.com/world/europe/humanitarian-corridor-set-allow-civilians-out-ukraines-sumy-deputy-pm-2022-03-08/], citing Vereshchuk. It comes after Ukraine rejected Russia’s proposal of humanitarian corridors into Russia and Belarus on Monday.

A planned 5-hour ceasefire in Mariupol that would allow civilians to seek safe passage on Saturday failed after Russian forces continued firing[https://www.theguardian.com/global/video/2022/mar/05/russia-fails-to-observe-ceasefire-in-mariupol-and-volnovakha-say-ukrainian-officials-video], Ukrainian officials said at the weekend.

Hundreds of international students, including from India and Nigeria, are also stuck[https://eu.usatoday.com/story/news/world/2022/03/05/ukraine-refugees-sumy-students/9382600002/] in Sumy and have told of running out[https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/mar/08/sumy-international-students-in-ukrainian-city-tell-of-desperation] of food and water.

At Least 1.2 Million Refugees From Ukraine Have Crossed Into Poland

At least 1.2 million refugees from Ukraine have entered Poland since the Russian invasion of their country began last month, the Polish Border Guard said[https://twitter.com/Straz_Graniczna/status/1501091352221732865] on Tuesday.

Some 141,500 refugees passed through the Polish border on Monday, the guard added. The number of Ukrainian refugees pouring into Poland may continue to rise as fighting in Ukraine continues to intensify.

As of Monday, more than 1.7 million refugees had left Ukraine, according to the United Nations. Besides Poland refugees have also streamed into Hungary, Slovakia, Moldova, Romania and Russia.

Venezuelan Leader Says He's Ready To Step Up Oil Production After 'Cordial' Meeting With U.S. Officials

Venezuelan President Nicolas Maduro on Monday indicated his willingness to increase his country’s oil production if the import of Russian oil is cut off, in a surprising move by the Russian ally.

According to the New York Times Maduro said that Venezuela has the capacity to produce more than 3 million barrels of crude oil per day “if necessary for the stability of the world.” The comments come after he engaged in what he called a “respectful, cordial, very diplomatic” meeting with U.S. officials over the weekend.

Venezuela’s oil industry is currently under heavy American sanctions and Maduro’s regime has close ties with Putin and Russia. However, Washington is now reportedly trying to court Maduro to help address rapidly rising crude oil prices by easing some of these sanctions.

The move is facing some resistance from U.S. lawmakers, including Bob Menendez[https://www.foreign.senate.gov/press/chair/release/chairman-menendez-statement-on-biden-administration-talks-with-the-maduro-regime] (D-N.J.) and Marco Rubio[https://twitter.com/marcorubio/status/1501038220712325122] (R-Fla.) who warn against propping up the Venezuelan strongman.

March 8, 2022 7:16 AM EST

U.S. Embassy Says Situation In Ukraine Remains Unpredictable

The U.S. Embassy in Ukraine on Tuesday stated[https://twitter.com/USEmbassyKyiv/status/1501056375614586881] that the security situation in Ukraine continues to remain “unpredictable” and warned Americans trying to flee the country to not cross into either Russia or Belarus.

The embassy noted that “conditions at each border can change very quickly and wait times can increase without warning.”

The advisory[https://travel.state.gov/content/travel/en/traveladvisories/ea/information-for-us-citizens-in-Ukraine.html] issued by the State Department warns Americans escaping Ukraine against traveling into Belarus due to “arbitrary enforcement of laws, the risk of detention.” U.S. citizens have also been advised against entering Russia as they may face “potential harassment...by Russian government security officials.”

Escaping U.S. citizens have been advised to go to Poland, Romania, Hungary, Slovakia and Moldova. The advisory however, urges them to avoid the pro-Russian separatist enclave of Transnistria in eastern Moldova.

Ukraine claims a Russian General Was Killed During Combat In Kharkiv

The Ukrainian military on Tuesday claimed that its forces have killed a Russian general near the besieged eastern city of Kharkiv.

The Ukrainian defense ministry identified him as Major General Vitaly Gerasimov, the first deputy commander of Russia's 41st army, claiming that he previously fought with Russian forces in Syria, Chechnya and Crimea.

If the claim is accurate, Gerasimov may be the second senior Russian commander to be killed in combat in Ukraine. Major General Andrei Sukhovetsky was killed by Ukrainian forces late last month, following the invasion.

While Sukhovetsky’s death has been acknowledged[https://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/europe/andrei-sukhovetsky-russian-general-killed-b2029363.html] by Russian officials, they has been no official statement on Gerasimov.
