U.K. Says Russia Is Targeting Civilians Evacuating Kyiv Suburb

The Russian military “continues to directly target” evacuation routes in the town of Irpin, killing multiple civilians who tried to flee the Kyiv suburb, the U.K. Ministry of Defense said in a tweet late Monday evening[https://twitter.com/DefenceHQ/status/1500981170280050688?ref_src=twsrc%5Egoogle%7Ctwcamp%5Eserp%7Ctwgr%5Etweet]. The report follows days of heavy[https://www.france24.com/en/live-news/20220307-hand-to-hand-combat-street-battles-rage-on-kyiv-s-edge] fighting[https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/2022/03/07/ukraine-kyiv-russia-advance/] between Ukrainian and Russian forces near Irpin, forcing residents to attempt to leave the town[https://www.reuters.com/world/europe/kyiv-digs-battle-fighting-flares-areas-nearby-2022-03-06/] while Russia[https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/2022/03/06/russian-forces-shelling-irpin/] launched[https://www.nytimes.com/2022/03/06/world/europe/ukraine-irpin-civilian-death.html] shells at[https://www.rferl.org/a/russia-ukraine-irpin-civilians-shelled/31739089.html] a route[https://www.cnn.com/2022/03/06/europe/ukraine-russia-invasion-sunday-intl-hnk/index.html] used by evacuating civilians Sunday.

The U.K. also said Ukrainian resistance “endures” near Irpin and in the nearby towns of Bucha, Hostomel and Vorzel. The fighting has left Irpin—a town of about 60,000[https://www.latimes.com/world-nation/story/2022-03-04/ukraine-irpin-evacuation-kyiv] people less than 15 miles outside Kyiv—without water or electricity for days, according to the Ministry of Defense.

Ukrainian and Russian officials have discussed establishing temporary, local ceasefires to allow residents of some cities to evacuate. Negotiations have been frayed, partly because Russian officials wants civilians to flee via “humanitarian corridors” into Russia and Belarus, an idea the Ukrainian government has cast as a propaganda effort[https://www.nbcnews.com/news/world/ukraine-rejects-russia-cease-fire-humanitarian-corridors-putin-belarus-rcna18936]. Ukraine is also wary of trusting the Russian military to abide by the terms of any local ceasefires, presidential advisor Mykhailo Podolyak told CNN last week.

March 8, 2022 2:29 AM EST

World Bank Approves $723 Million Support Package For Ukraine

The World Bank’s board of directors approved a $723 million support package[https://www.worldbank.org/en/news/press-release/2022/03/07/world-bank-mobilizes-an-emergency-financing-package-of-over-700-million-for-ukraine] to aid Ukraine following Russia’s invasion, the institution announced Monday. The package brings together loans, loan guarantees, grants and parallel financing from several countries, and the World Bank says it will help pay for “critical services” in Ukraine like hospital wages and social programs.

The World Bank says it is preparing another $3 billion package for Ukraine “in the coming months,” in addition to aid for surrounding countries that are receiving millions of Ukrainian refugees.

The World Bank has committed nearly $14 billion[https://www.worldbank.org/en/country/ukraine/overview#2] to Ukraine since the country first joined the organization in 1992, which was shortly after the fall of the Soviet Union.

U.S. House Speaker Nancy Pelosi (D-Calif.) announced[https://www.speaker.gov/newsroom/3622] Sunday night Congress is aiming to approve $10 billion in support for Ukraine, a sum initially requested by the White House[https://www.whitehouse.gov/wp-content/uploads/2022/03/COVID-and-Ukraine-Supplemental-Funding-Request-Pelosi.pdf] to pay for military and humanitarian assistance as well as extra Pentagon spending. It adds to over $650 million[https://www.forbes.com/sites/annakaplan/2022/02/25/live-us-will-sanction-putin-for-russian-invasion-of-ukraine/?sh=78d19ecc1d2c] in security aid and $50 million in humanitarian aid already sent to Ukraine by the United States over the last year.

Ukraine Infrastructure Minister Oleksander Kubrakov said Monday in a televised address Russia’s invasion had already destroyed $10 billion worth of the country’s infrastructure, and it will take years to fix the damage inflicted on many of Ukraine’s cities by Russian shelling, according[https://www.reuters.com/world/europe/ukrainian-minister-puts-war-damage-infrastructure-about-10-billion-2022-03-07/] to Reuters.

March 8, 2022 1:51 AM EST

Shelling Damages Nuclear Research Facility In Kharkiv

The International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA) reported[https://www.iaea.org/newscenter/pressreleases/update-14-iaea-director-general-statement-on-situation-in-ukraine] Monday a nuclear research facility in Ukraine’s second-largest city of Kharkiv was damaged by shelling, though radiation levels at the site did not increase, according to Ukraine’s nuclear regulator. This facility—which IAEA said produces “radioisotopes for medical and industrial applications”—did not have a large inventory of radioactive material, so there is little risk of disaster, IAEA Director General Rafael Grossi said in a statement.

However, the IAEA says it remains concerned about the state of Ukraine’s four nuclear power plants[https://news.un.org/en/story/2022/03/1113382] amid Russia’s invasion. The Zaporizhzhya Nuclear Power Plant, the country’s largest, is now under Russia’s control after Russian forces seized it last week, starting a fire at a nearby training facility in the process—an act the U.S. embassy in Kyiv deemed[https://twitter.com/USEmbassyKyiv/status/1499708231903547394?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw%7Ctwcamp%5Etweetembed%7Ctwterm%5E1499708231903547394%7Ctwgr%5E%7Ctwcon%5Es1_&ref_url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cbsnews.com%2Flive-updates%2Frussia-ukraine-power-plant-fire-war-news-latest-vladimir-putin-invasion%2F] a war crime. The plant was not damaged during the fire, but U.S. officials said[https://www.reuters.com/world/china/un-security-council-hold-emergency-meeting-after-russian-attack-nuclear-plant-2022-03-04/] the attack could’ve easily led to a nuclear disaster. The IAEA said Monday it is not possible to get medicine or spare parts to the Zaporizhzhya plant, adding to earlier concerns that staff are overworked and outside communication has largely been cut off. Grossi noted all of these factors pose safety risks.

Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky posted a video[https://www.facebook.com/zelenskiy.official/posts/3026120197638336?__cft__[0]=AZUCUZwH1pXjhdPvOKe59ldMJjx_3mLfxP9QgRDI5Rmw8Kh-0JWv-BKnXTrGW2qcjtsN3_Jj0MyBMVYJizK3H6OmHDPi97A2rZBHCUNIEFGqpp0s1WCFXi-gwl7BNtXy5ucbgmE0l1HB4NGhzYw9X0la&__tn__=%2CO%2CP-R] of himself in his office in Kyiv late Monday night, the first time Zelensky has appeared in the aboveground space since Russia’s invasion of Ukraine began almost two weeks ago, CNN and The Guardian noted.

“I stay in Kyiv. On Bankhova Street. I’m not hiding. And I'm not afraid of anyone,” Zelensky said in the video, according to a translation[https://www.president.gov.ua/en/news/kozhen-den-sprotivu-stvoryuye-dlya-ukrayini-krashi-umovi-na-73417] released by his office that referenced the presidential administration building’s location on Bankhova Street in central Kyiv.

Zelensky has declined U.S. offers to evacuate him from Ukraine’s capital city as Russian ground troops loom just miles away, at one point telling American officials he needs “ammunition, not a ride,” the Associated Press[https://apnews.com/article/russia-ukraine-volodymyr-zelenskyy-kyiv-poland-europe-c270027c825e009ed66988bb98a3476e] reported. Still, Zelensky has acknowledged threats to his safety, as Western officials warn Russia is likely angling to oust[https://www.state.gov/secretary-antony-j-blinken-with-james-landale-of-the-bbc/] the Ukrainian government. He called[https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/2022/02/25/russia-ukraine-president-zelensky-family-target/] himself[https://www.wsj.com/articles/once-a-comic-ukrainian-president-transforms-into-wartime-leader-11645794378] “target number one” for Russia shortly after the invasion began, and he reportedly told[https://www.axios.com/zelensky-eu-leaders-last-time-you-see-me-alive-3447dbc0-620d-4ccc-afad-082e81d7a29f.html] European Union officials during a call last week it could be the last time they see him alive.

March 7, 2022 10:36 PM EST

Russia Claims To Offer Ceasefire For 5 Ukrainian Cities

Russian state media claims a temporary ceasefire could start Tuesday to allow evacuations from five of Ukraine's biggest and hardest-hit cities.

The ceasefire would start at 2 a.m. Eastern time Tuesday morning in the cities of Kyiv, Kharkiv, Mariupol, Chernihiv and Sumy, Russian outlets Interfax and TASS reported Monday, citing the Russian government. As part of the arrangement, Russian officials are reportedly requiring[https://www.cnn.com/europe/live-news/ukraine-russia-putin-news-03-07-22/h_8f05450550521508b6a64cec1d4dff3a] Ukraine to agree to let residents flee via "humanitarian corridors" into Russian and Belarusian territory, which Ukrainian officials have derided as[https://www.nbcnews.com/news/world/ukraine-rejects-russia-cease-fire-humanitarian-corridors-putin-belarus-rcna18936] "propaganda corridors."

Ukraine did not appear to immediately respond to the ceasefire offer, and it's unclear what other terms Russia might be demanding. Ukrainian officials reportedly have until 7 p.m. Eastern time Monday to accept the offer.

The two sides last week agreed in principle to set up humanitarian corridors, giving survivors of besieged cities a chance of fleeing to safety, but Ukrainian officials have questioned Russia's proposals so far. Ukraine rebuffed a similar offer[https://www.reuters.com/markets/asia/top-wrap-1-ukrainians-trapped-besieged-city-fighting-blocks-evacuation-efforts-2022-03-07/] to set up “humanitarian corridors” in four cities—including Kyiv—earlier Monday, and an attempted ceasefire to evacuate the cities of Mariupol and Volnovakha on Saturday collapsed within hours.

Ukrainian presidential advisor Mykhailo Podolyak said Monday talks with Russian officials yielded "small positive" progress in improving the logistics of humanitarian corridors.

Ukraine President Zelensky Will Address U.K. Parliament

Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky will deliver a speech to the U.K. House of Commons at 12 p.m. Eastern time on Tuesday, according to a statement from House Speaker Lindsay Hoyle.

The address will take place through a video link, which will mark the first time an address has been given to the chamber via video, according to the statement. There will be no questions-and-answers session following Zelensky's speech.

"Every parliamentarian wants to hear directly from the president, who will be speaking to us live from Ukraine, so this is an important opportunity for the House," Hoyle said.

Western countries like the U.K. have faced growing calls to put further sanctions on Russia, such as a ban on the import of Russian oil, though Prime Minister Boris Johnson expressed caution[https://www.theguardian.com/business/live/2022/mar/07/markets-slide-surging-oil-price-stagflation-ftse-russia-sanctions-business-live] when pressed on the issue Monday. Ukraine Claims It Has Suffered $10 Billion In Damage So Far

Ukraine Infrastructure Minister Oleksander Kubrakov said Monday the Russian invasion has already inflicted around $10 billion in damage to the nation's infrastructure, as shelling continues around the country while Russian troops advance on larger cities.

March 7, 2022 5:00 PM EST

UK, Germany Push Back On Russian Oil Ban

German Chancellor Olaf Scholz and British Prime Minister Boris Johnson expressed hesitation on a ban of Russian oil Monday, a day after Secretary of State Antony Blinken said[https://www.forbes.com/sites/dereksaul/2022/03/06/us-in-active-discussions-to-ban-russian-oil-blinken-says/?sh=44089d903545] the U.S. is in “very active discussions with our European partners” on a ban.

Scholz said[https://www.theguardian.com/business/live/2022/mar/07/markets-slide-surging-oil-price-stagflation-ftse-russia-sanctions-business-live?filterKeyEvents=false&page=with:block-622609a08f087f6d099f28cc#block-622609a08f087f6d099f28cc] in a Monday statement[https://www.cbsnews.com/newyork/live-updates/russia-ukraine-news-war-vladimir-putin-nato-fighter-jets/] he opposes a blanket ban, saying Russian oil is of “essential importance” and Germany will “continue the activities of business enterprises in the area of energy supply with Russia.” The European Union hasn’t publicly weighed in on a ban, and Bloomberg reports[https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2022-03-07/eu-is-divided-over-russian-oil-sanctions-as-calls-for-ban-grow] its members are split on the decision.

Johnson similarly noted the logistical challenges of a ban, saying[https://www.theguardian.com/business/live/2022/mar/07/markets-slide-surging-oil-price-stagflation-ftse-russia-sanctions-business-live], "You can’t simply close down use of oil and gas overnight, even from Russia." However, Johnson was less resolute on ruling out a ban, saying it remains[https://www.reuters.com/world/all-options-table-uk-pm-says-when-asked-about-russian-oil-ban-2022-03-07/] “on the table.”

Russia accounts for about 40%[https://www.wsj.com/articles/europe-fears-it-could-be-too-late-to-shake-off-russian-gas-addiction-11646476200#:~:text=The%20European%20Union%20gets%20around,has%20grown%20in%20recent%20years.] of the EU gas supply and about 4%[https://www.gov.uk/government/news/russia-ukraine-and-uk-energy-factsheet#:~:text=There%20are%20no%20gas%20pipelines,UK%20gas%20supply%20in%202021.] of the UK’s. Brent crude briefly hit a 14-year high of $139.13 early Monday, but quickly fell, trading at $123.08 at 12 ET Monday.

— Derek Saul

UN Says More Than 1,200 Civilian Casualties So Far

* Since Russia invaded Ukraine February 24, 406 civilians have died and 801 more were injured, the Office of the United Nations High Commissioner for Human Rights (OHCHR) said in a Monday statement[https://www.ohchr.org/EN/NewsEvents/Pages/DisplayNews.aspx?NewsID=28179&LangID=E].

* The OHCHR “believes that the real figures are considerably higher,” as reports of further casualties await confirmation.

* The OHCHR said 27 children have died in the war so far.

* Civilian casualties were most densely concentrated in the Donetsk and Luhansk regions of eastern Ukraine, with 93 civilians dead and 435 more injured.

* Ukraine has yet to release an official civilian death total, but the OHCHR noted it is working to verify reports from the Ukraine government of hundreds of additional civilian casualties in separate incidents Sunday and Monday.

March 7, 2022 2:00 PM EST

Russia Issues Demands Of Ukraine—The Same Ones From Before Invasion

Kremlin Press Secretary Dmitry Peskov told[https://www.reuters.com/world/kremlin-says-russian-military-action-will-stop-moment-if-ukraine-meets-2022-03-07/] Reuters Monday it will stop its war in Ukraine “in a moment” should Ukraine meet its lengthy set of demands, which includes recognizing the Russian-backed Donetsk and Luhansk rebel states in eastern Ukraine as independent, the Crimean peninsula seized by Russia in 2014 as Russian territory, and amending its constitution to commit to neutrality, promising never to join a group such as NATO.

The demands Peskov outlined Monday match hose Russia asked for before its full-scale invasion. Peskov parroted Russian President Vladimir Putin’s comments leading up to the war, claiming Russia was forced into the invasion due to Ukraine and the West’s actions.

Peskov claimed Russia was pushed into invasion because of an impending invasion of eastern Ukraine—an unfounded claim—saying "what tipped the balance was the lives of these 3 million people” and believing “they would be attacked." Russia has consistently advanced baseless claims[https://theconversation.com/putins-claims-that-ukraine-is-committing-genocide-are-baseless-but-not-unprecedented-177511] of genocide against Russian speakers in eastern Ukraine.

The United States and European Union sanctioned[https://www.forbes.com/sites/annakaplan/2022/03/03/us-sanctions-7-russian-oligarchs-and-kremlin-press-secretary-peskov/?sh=ff0a58c6b6ea] Peskov last week, and the White House called him a “top purveyor of Putin’s propaganda” in its announcement of the penalties.

March 7, 2022 11:34 AM EST

Putin Will Not Stop In Ukraine, Lithuanian President Tells Blinken

Lithuanian President Gitanas Nauseda on Monday warned U.S. Secretary of State Antony Blinken that Russia’s invasion of Ukraine could spiral into a global conflict and urged him to do more to help Kyiv.

Nauseda told Blinken, who is on a tour of the Baltic states, that Vladimir Putin “will not stop in Ukraine” and the world had a duty to help Ukrainians by “all means available,” Reuters reported[https://www.reuters.com/world/europe/putin-will-not-stop-ukraine-lithuanian-president-warns-blinken-2022-03-07/].

“I mean indeed all means if we want to avoid the Third World War. The choice is in our hands,” Nauseda said. He added that the policy of deterrence was no longer adequate and “forward defense” was now needed.

Blinken reiterated that NATO was defensive alliance and stressed that U.S. commitment to NATO's mutual defense pact is “sacrosanct.” He added: We will defend every inch of NATO territory if it comes under attack. No one should doubt our readiness, no one should doubt our resolve.”

Blinken was set to visit neighboring Baltic nations of Latvia and Estonia later on Monday and Tuesday respectively.

Ukraine Dismisses Russia's Offer For A Humanitarian Corridor For Fleeing Civilians

Moscow on Monday announced new “humanitarian corridors” for Ukrainian civilians that would lead them into the territory of Russia and its ally Belarus, an offer that was immediately rebuked by Kyiv who called it an “immoral stunt.”

The new “corridors” would be opened at 10 a.m. Moscow time on Monday from the capital Kyiv and the cities of Kharkiv, Sumy and Mariupol, Russia's defense ministry said.

Maps of these corridors[https://twitter.com/maria_avdv/status/1500754226422726657] published by Russian state controlled outlet RIA showed the corridor from Kyiv leading to Belarus, while the corridors out of Kharkiv, Sumy and Mariupol only lead to Russia.

According to[https://www.reuters.com/markets/asia/top-wrap-1-ukrainians-trapped-besieged-city-fighting-blocks-evacuation-efforts-2022-03-07/] Reuters, a spokesperson for Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky dismissed the offer as “completely immoral” and added that the citizens of Ukraine should have the right to “evacuate to the territory of Ukraine.”

March 7, 2022 8:30 AM EST

Russia Is Targeting Ukraine's Communication Infrastructure, U.K. Defense Ministry Says

The Russian military is likely targeting Ukraine’s communications infrastructure in an effort to prevent the country’s citizens from accessing reliable news and information, the U.K. Defense Ministry said in its daily intelligence update[https://twitter.com/DefenceHQ/status/1500727508014088192] on the situation in Ukraine on Monday.

The report notes that Russia struck a TV tower in Kharkiv on Sunday, halting broadcasts.

The update also warns that Ukraine’s internet access is highly likely to be disrupted due to collateral damage on infrastructure caused by Russian air strikes. So far, internet outages have been reported in Mariupol, Sumy, Kyiv and Kharkiv.

Ukrainians have effectively used the internet and social media to rally global support for their cause, while Russia’s cyber efforts have been severely hamstrung as U.S. internet giants have cracked down Moscow-backed outlets and individuals.

Moscow Is Reportedly Recruiting Syrian Fighters For Urban Combat In Ukraine

Russia is recruiting Syrian fighters, experienced in urban combat, to fight in Ukraine as its forces move closer into major cities, the Wall Street Journal reports[https://www.wsj.com/articles/russia-recruiting-syrians-for-urban-combat-in-ukraine-u-s-officials-say-11646606234], citing unnamed U.S. officials.

Russia has been fighting in Syria in support of Bashar al-Assad’s regime since 2015. Moscow reportedly believes that to tap into the fighters’ expertise in urban combat could help capture Kyiv.

Last week, Syrian publication DeirEzzor24 reported[https://deirezzor24.net/en/russia-has-opened-the-door-to-volunteering-in-eastern-syria-to-recruit-mercenaries-and-send-them-to-ukraine/] that Russia has offered volunteers between $200 and $300 “to go to Ukraine and operate as guards” for six months at a time.

New Zealand Government Working On Legislation That Would Allow It To Sanction Russia

New Zealand’s government is working to swiftly pass legislation that will allow it to join Western sanctions against Russia.

So far, the South Pacific nation has not been part of a multinational effort to impose economic sanctions on Moscow outside of voting in the U.N. as it lacked the legal means to do so.

Prime Minister Jacinda Ardern on Monday said that the new laws would enable her government to target people, companies and assets linked to the invasion. Ardern also noted that New Zealand may also freeze assets linked to Russian oligarchs and block their superyachts or planes from arriving.

“The creation of this targeted sanctions bill will send a very clear signal that New Zealand will not be a safe haven for those wishing to move their investments here.”— New Zealand Foreign Minister Nanaia Mahuta

March 6, 2022 9:36 PM EST

Russia Shuts Off Communication From Ukrainian Nuclear Power Plant, IAEA Says

The International Atomic Energy Agency reported[https://www.iaea.org/newscenter/pressreleases/update-13-iaea-director-general-statement-on-situation-in-ukraine] Sunday Russian forces have switched off phone lines, emails and fax at the Zaporizhzhya Nuclear Power Plant, which Russia took control of last week, further threatening the stability of Ukraine’s largest nuclear power plant.

“Reliable communications between the regulator and the operator are a critical part of overall nuclear safety and security,” Director General of the IAEA Rafael Grossi said in a release Sunday.

The update, based on the IAEA’s communication with Ukraine’s nuclear regulator, said mobile phone communication was still available, “but with poor quality.”

The IAEA said the plant is now under the command of the Russian military leader that took the plant last week and alleged that plant management, made up of Ukrainian staff who have been working in inhumane conditions, must seek approval from the commander before taking any action.

March 6, 2022 7:41 PM EST

American Express Suspends Operations In Russia And Belarus, Joining Visa, Mastercard

American Express is suspending all operations in Russia in response to the country’s “unjustified attack on the people of Ukraine,” the credit card company announced Sunday in a release[https://about.americanexpress.com/all-news/news-details/2022/American-Express-Suspends-Operations-in-Russia-and-Belarus/default.aspx]. Globally issued American Express cards will no longer work at merchants or ATMs in Russia, and cards issued in Russia by Russian banks will no longer work internationally. The company said it is also ending all business operations in Belarus.

“We stand by our colleagues, customers, and the international community in hoping for a peaceful resolution to this crisis,” the company said.

American Express joins other major credit card companies Visa and Mastercard, along with dozens of other corporations, in a stance against Russia following its invasion of Ukraine. Visa and Mastercard released statements announcing their respective suspensions of service in Russia within 16 minutes of each other Saturday, according[https://apnews.com/article/russia-ukraine-business-europe-9c0554f52e25088ae6e7b492cb04e6d3] to the Associated Press.

“When it is appropriate, and if it is permissible under the law, we will…work to restore operations,” Mastercard announced in a release[https://www.mastercard.com/news/press/2022/march/mastercard-statement-on-suspension-of-russian-operations/], confirming its cards would no longer work in Russia, and cards issued by Russian banks will not be supported on the Mastercard network internationally.

“We are compelled to act following Russia’s unprovoked invasion of Ukraine, and the unacceptable events that we have witnessed,” Visa chairman and CEO Al Kelly said[https://usa.visa.com/about-visa/newsroom/press-releases.releaseId.18871.html] in the company’s release, announcing the same changes.

Surprised By Ukrainian Resistance, Russia Is Targeting Populated Areas: U.K. Defense Ministry

The update notes that Russia’s supply lines continue to be targeted, slowing the speed of its advance.

The update adds that there is a “realistic possibility” that the Russians are attempting to disguise their fuel trucks as regular support vehicles to minimize losses.

March 6, 2022 1:10 AM EST

U.S. Eyes Deal In Which Poland Would Provide Warplanes To Ukraine In Exchange For F-16s

The United States is in discussions to supply Poland with F-16 fighter jets in return for the former Soviet Bloc country sending its aging MiG-29s to Ukraine, U.S. officials told Politico[https://www.politico.com/news/2022/03/05/white-house-deal-fighter-jets-ukraine-00014424] and the Wall Street Journal[https://www.wsj.com/articles/ukrainian-president-asks-congress-to-help-secure-russian-made-jet-fighters-11646511532?mod=djemalertNEWS] late Saturday, after Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky made an impassioned plea on a video call earlier in the day with U.S. lawmakers for Russian-made warplanes to help his country fight off a Russian invasion.

An EU promise last week to provide used Russian fighter jets to Ukraine fell through[https://www.politico.com/news/2022/02/28/ukrainian-pilots-arrive-in-poland-to-pick-up-donated-fighter-jets-00012560] after member countries possessing the planes balked.

Poland has been updating its air force: starting in 2006 it acquired 48 F-16s that serve as its main frontline fighter, and in 2020 it inked a $4.6 billion contract to buy 32 F-35s. But it still has about two dozen old MiG-29 twin-engine fighters — a type that Ukraine’s air force also flies. Poland is reportedly asking the U.S. to sell it more F-16s to make up the difference if it hands the planes over to Ukraine.

To the surprise of many military experts[https://rusi.org/explore-our-research/publications/rusi-defence-systems/russian-air-force-actually-incapable-complex-air-operations], Russia has so far failed to establish air superiority over Ukraine, despite on paper possessing a far superior air force and air-defense systems. Ukraine still has a “significant majority” of its military aircraft available, a U.S. defense official said Friday.

But Ukraine still appears overmatched, and Zelenksy has repeatedly asked the U.S. and NATO to establish a no-fly zone over Ukraine, something Western officials have said is out of the question given the likelihood that would lead to a wider war with Russia. On Saturday, he reportedly told U.S. lawmakers, “if you can’t do that, at least get me planes.”

Mastercard And Visa Suspend Operations In Russia Over Invasion Of Ukraine

Mastercard and Visa are suspending operations in Russia with its invasion of Ukraine stretching into a 10th day, the two companies said Saturday, deepening the country’s economic isolation following a wave of sanctions.

Mastercard said in a statement[https://www.mastercard.com/news/press/2022/march/mastercard-statement-on-suspension-of-russian-operations/] that cards issued by Russian banks will no longer be supported by the Mastercard network and that any Mastercards issued outside of Russia will not work in Russia or ATMs there.

Visa said in a statement[https://investor.visa.com/news/news-details/2022/Visa-Suspends-All-Russia-Operations/default.aspx] it will work with clients and partners in Russia to stop all Visa transactions in the coming days. It added that all transactions initiated with Visa cards issued in Russia will no longer work outside of the country and any Visa cards issued outside of Russia will no longer work in Russia.

Mastercard and Visa cards accounted for nearly 74%[https://www.wsj.com/articles/russian-consumers-cut-off-from-many-foreign-purchases-11646154897] of card payment transactions in Russia in 2020, according to the Nilson Report.

Russia has restarted offensive operations in Mariupol and Volnovakha, the two southern cities where Ukrainian and Russian officials arranged to allow civilians to evacuate through humanitarian corridors as part of a temporary ceasefire agreement that collapsed hours after it began, with the U.K.’s Ministry of Defense accusing Russia of abusing the agreement to reset its forces.

“Due to the unwillingness of the Ukrainian side to influence the nationalists or to extend the ceasefire, offensive operations have been resumed from 18:00 Moscow time,” Maj. Gen. Igor Konashenkov, a spokesperson for Russia’s Defense Ministry, said in a statement to Russian news agency Tass. Konashenkov claimed “not a single civilian” was able to leave the two cities through the corridors because “nationalists” prevented them from leaving.

However, in an intelligence update[https://twitter.com/DefenceHQ/status/1500159409976131584/photo/1], the U.K.’s Ministry of Defense alleged[https://twitter.com/DefenceHQ/status/1500159409976131584/photo/1] Russia likely agreed to the ceasefire in Mariupol only to provide cover to reset some of its forces for a continued offensive attack without receiving “international condemnation.”

“By accusing Ukraine of breaking the agreement, Russia is likely seeking to shift responsibility for current and future civilian casualties in the city,” it said.

The evacuation process for children, women and the elderly to escape the fighting in the two cities in southern Ukraine was postponed[https://twitter.com/Pavlo_Kyrylenko/status/1500061621330362374] hours after it began, after Ukrainian officials said[https://www.facebook.com/100005295963148/videos/271886048436286/] Russia broke the agreement by shelling the cities and nearby areas along the evacuation routes.

March 5, 2022 6:16 PM EST

U.N. Counts 351 Civilian Deaths In Ukraine As Russian Attack Continues

The United Nations’ human rights office said[https://www.ohchr.org/EN/NewsEvents/Pages/DisplayNews.aspx?NewsID=28179&LangID=E] Saturday 351 civilians had died and 707 had been injured as of Friday since Russia’s invasion of Ukraine started last week, noting that the actual figures were likely “considerably higher.”

The figures include 329 adult deaths and 22 child deaths, and 671 adult injuries and 36 child injuries.

The U.N. has said the actual numbers were undoubtedly much higher given difficulties in reporting and corroborating casualties amid the ongoing fighting. The U.N. alleged "hundreds of civilian casualties” in Volnovakha, the Ukrainian government-controlled area of Donetsk.

Most civilian casualties continued to be caused by “ explosive[https://www.ohchr.org/EN/NewsEvents/Pages/DisplayNews.aspx?NewsID=28179&LangID=E] weapons with a wide impact area,” including shelling from heavy artillery, multi-launch rocket systems and missiles and airstrikes, the U.N. said.

Armed resistance to Russian forces has ended in the strategically important southern Ukrainian city of Kherson, where the city’s mayor told CNN[https://www.cnn.com/europe/live-news/ukraine-russia-putin-news-03-05-22/index.html] the local population was unarmed, and a large protest was staged Saturday morning, according to the New York Times[https://www.nytimes.com/live/2022/03/05/world/russia-ukraine/kherson-protests-ukraine].

“We don’t have more weapons to resist, to put up an armed resistance,” said Mayor Ihor Kolykhaiev, who told CNN the army had retreated toward Nikolaev.

A senior U.S. defense official told reporters as recently as Friday the Pentagon could not independently verify reports[https://www.economist.com/europe/2022/03/02/a-regional-capital-in-ukraine-falls-to-russia-for-the-first-time] Russian forces had control of Kherson, as there were still clashes in and around the city.

Saturday morning, a crowd that Kolykhaiev estimated at 2,000 gathered in the city center to protest the Russian occupation of the city.

Kolykhaiev called for humanitarian assistance as “all city services are down,” including water and power. He noted logistical issues with receiving the aid, as the only route to receive supplies is through Crimea, which has been Russian-controlled since its annexation in 2014. The citizens of Kherson also only want to take aid from Ukrainians, according to Kolykhaiev.

“Russians want to send their humanitarian aid, but the people of Kherson are refusing it, because they are patriots and they don’t want aid from Russians,” he said. “So we are not receiving humanitarian aid at the moment.”

March 5, 2022 3:10 PM EST

Resistance Of Russian Invasion Of Ukraine Calls ‘Into Question’ Its Statehood, Putin Says

Russian President Vladimir Putin said Saturday that Ukraine is at risk of losing its statehood if officials continued to resist his invasion of the country, and denied that Russian conscripts are involved in the fighting.

Claiming there was a possibility of nuclear weapons being placed in Ukraine, Putin said, “Those from today's leadership should understand that if they continue doing what they are doing, they call into question the future of Ukrainian statehood. And if it happens it will be wholly and fully on their conscience,” Interfax quoted[https://interfax.com/newsroom/top-stories/75628/] Putin as saying during a meeting Saturday with female crew members of Russian airlines.

In his first extended remarks since the war began, Putin claimed Russia would not need to conscript soldiers to help fight in the invasion, nor would the country need to impose martial law. There have been multiple reports[https://www.washingtonpost.com/politics/2022/03/01/russia-low-morale-ukraine-invasion/] from Russian soldiers and family members of servicemen[https://meduza.io/en/feature/2022/02/26/i-m-panicking-where-is-my-child] that conscripts are being put into action in Ukraine, though the military is not legally allowed to employ them in combat.

“Only professional military servicemen take part in this operation, officers and contract soldiers,” Putin claimed, according[https://www.nytimes.com/live/2022/03/05/world/russia-ukraine] to the New York Times. “Not a single conscript takes part in it, and we don’t plan to send them there.”

Putin said he did not plan to impose martial law in Russia, unless other countries became aggressive toward the country, including implementing a no-fly zone, which Ukrainian officials have repeatedly[https://www.nytimes.com/2022/03/04/world/europe/zelensky-nato-no-fly-zone-ukraine.html] called for. “The realization of that demand would bring catastrophic results not only to Europe, but to the whole world,” he said.

Western nations have rebuffed[https://www.whitehouse.gov/briefing-room/press-briefings/2022/03/03/press-briefing-by-press-secretary-jen-psaki-march-3rd-2022/] the calls for a no-fly zone due to the likelihood that it would lead to a wider war with Russia.

In the meeting, Putin also attacked the sanctions[https://www.forbes.com/sites/annakaplan/2022/03/03/us-sanctions-7-russian-oligarchs-and-kremlin-press-secretary-peskov/] countries have imposed on Russia in retaliation for invading Ukraine, describing[https://interfax.com/newsroom/top-stories/75630/] them as “like the declaration of war.”

March 5, 2022 4:13 AM EST

Singapore Unveils Rare Slate Of Sanctions Against Russian Banks And Tech Imports

Singapore will restrict military and high-tech exports to Russia and sanction four Russian banks, including VTB, the Singaporean government announced[https://www.mfa.gov.sg/Newsroom/Press-Statements-Transcripts-and-Photos/2022/03/20220305-sanctions] Saturday morning, an unusual move for a country that normally doesn’t impose sanctions on its own. Singapore is also prohibiting financial institutions from helping Russia’s Central Bank or the Russian government raise funds, and barring the use of cryptocurrency to evade sanctions.

Officials in Singapore first indicated[https://www.cnbc.com/2022/02/28/singapore-to-impose-sanctions-on-russia-including-bank-transactions.html] they will roll out sanctions against Russia on Monday. Singapore usually does not enforce sanctions against foreign countries unless they are approved by the United Nations Security Council, Reuters[https://www.reuters.com/world/asia-pacific/singapore-impose-appropriate-sanctions-restrictions-russia-2022-02-28/] and Bloomberg[https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2022-02-28/singapore-to-sanction-russia-in-almost-unprecedented-move] noted earlier this week. But the government has argued Russia’s invasion of Ukraine is uniquely worrisome because, as a tiny island nation, Singapore views respecting borders as sacrosanct.

“While we continue to value good relations with Russia and the Russian people, we cannot accept the Russian government’s violation of the sovereignty and territorial integrity of another sovereign state. For a small state like Singapore, this is not a theoretical principle, but a dangerous precedent,” the government said in a statement Saturday.

Dozens of other countries have enacted financial penalties similar to Singapore’s. The United States and United Kingdom sanctioned[https://www.forbes.com/sites/joewalsh/2022/02/24/biden-announces-new-sanctions-on-russian-banks-and-tech-imports-following-ukraine-invasion/?sh=418464da5d8a] VTB—Russia’s second-largest bank—last week, and U.S., U.K. and European Union leaders plan to halt some technology exports to Russia.

Zelensky Advisor: Ukraine Working To Create 'Humanitarian Corridors'—But Trusting Russia Could Be Difficult

One day after Ukraine and Russia tentatively agreed[https://www.forbes.com/sites/forbesstaffreports/2022/03/04/live-ukraine-russia/?sh=12e5aaf01519] to create “humanitarian corridors” to access besieged Ukrainian cities, an advisor to Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky told CNN Friday evening “the work on the logistics is now in progress” to set up temporary routes to evacuate civilians and resupply certain cities with food and medicine.

Zelensky’s advisor Mykhailo Podolyak told CNN Russia hasn’t agreed to create permanent humanitarian corridors, but Ukraine hopes to establish temporary routes that could be free of fighting for several hours at a time.

Podolyak also acknowledged it will be difficult to trust Russia’s military to respect ceasefires near agreed-upon corridors because “its current tactics [are] aimed at spreading panic among people.” Russia has ramped up bombardments of cities like Kyiv and Kharkiv in recent days, destroying apartment complexes and endangering civilians. He suggested international organizations could act as mediators to monitor the status of any humanitarian corridors.

— Joe Walsh

March 5, 2022 12:01 AM EST

CNN Stops Broadcasting In Russia Amid Kremlin Media Crackdown

CNN will halt broadcasting in Russia, the cable network said Friday, making it the latest Western news outlet to scale back its presence in the country amid tightening government restrictions while it prosecutes an invasion of Ukraine.

A CNN spokesperson told Forbes the network “will stop broadcasting in Russia while we continue to evaluate the situation and our next steps moving forward.” A source at CNN said the outlet doesn’t plan on pulling its staff out of Russia.

The Russian government has sought to tightly control how media outlets report on the war with Ukraine, passing a law that threatens journalists with prison sentences of up to 15 years if they report what the Kremlin deems to be fake information about the invasion. Two of the country’s only remaining independent broadcasters left[https://www.nytimes.com/2022/03/03/world/europe/russia-ukraine-propaganda-censorship.html] the airwaves[https://www.niemanlab.org/2022/03/russia-blocks-tv-rain-its-last-independent-tv-channel-and-tv-rain-airs-its-last-broadcast/] earlier this week amid pressure[https://www.cnn.com/2022/03/02/media/journalism-crackdown-russia/index.html] from Russian authorities. Earlier Friday, access to Facebook[https://www.forbes.com/sites/forbesstaffreports/2022/03/04/live-ukraine-russia/?sh=68304b321519] and the BBC’s website was blocked, and the BBC said[https://www.bbc.com/news/world-europe-60617365] it would suspend local reporting in Russia.

March 4, 2022 11:52 PM EST

Zelensky Excoriates NATO For Rejecting No-Fly Zone Over Ukraine

Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky said NATO “gave the green light for further bombing of Ukrainian cities and villages” by refusing to implement a no-fly zone over his country, in a speech late Friday night translated by Reuters[https://www.reuters.com/business/aerospace-defense/nato-meets-ukraine-calls-no-fly-zone-hinder-russia-2022-03-04/]. Zelensky also called a summit of NATO countries earlier Friday “weak” and “confused,” pushing the alliance to take further action[https://www.cnn.com/europe/live-news/ukraine-russia-putin-news-03-04-22/index.html].

As Russia escalates its bombardment of Ukrainian cities, Zelensky has repeatedly urged Western leaders to force Russian warplanes to stay out of Ukraine’s airspace. The United States and its allies have categorically[https://www.whitehouse.gov/briefing-room/press-briefings/2022/03/03/press-briefing-by-press-secretary-jen-psaki-march-3rd-2022/] rejected[https://twitter.com/PentagonPresSec/status/1498450940512382980] the idea[https://www.cnn.com/2022/03/04/politics/blinken-stoltenberg-no-fly-zone-warning/index.html] and warned enforcing a no-fly zone would require Western militaries to confront and shoot down Russian aircraft, leading to the risk of a broader war between nuclear-armed powers. NATO Secretary-General Jens Stoltenberg said Friday[https://www.nato.int/cps/en/natohq/news_192795.htm] “we have a responsibility to ensure [the war] does not escalate and spread beyond Ukraine. That would be even more devastating and dangerous.”

Beyond the risks[https://warontherocks.com/2022/03/the-dangerous-allure-of-the-no-fly-zone/], some military experts argue[https://warontherocks.com/2022/03/the-dangerous-allure-of-the-no-fly-zone/] a no-fly zone’s impact on the war in Ukraine could be fairly muted, since most Ukrainian civilian deaths so far appear to have been caused by artillery fire and missile strikes rather than attacks from aircraft.

“Contrary to what so many in the commentariat seem to believe, a no-fly zone is not a military half-measure. It is a combat operation designed to deprive the enemy of its airpower, and it involves direct and sustained fighting,” the national security website War on the Rocks wrote.

March 4, 2022 10:49 PM EST

White House ‘Looking At Options’ To Limit Russian Oil—But Blinken Finds ‘No Strategic Interest’ In Supply Cuts

The federal government is considering ways to reduce U.S. imports of Russian oil, Council of Economic Advisors Chair Cecilia Rouse said Friday, though the Biden Administration still appears hesitant to pursue a strategy that could cause prices to spike even higher.

Rouse said during a White House press briefing “we are looking at options that we can take right now if we were to cut the U.S. consumption of Russian energy, but what’s really most important is that we maintain a steady supply of global energy.”

However, Secretary of State Antony Blinken pushed back on the idea of restricting Russian oil Friday, telling reporters “there’s no strategic interest in reducing the global supply of energy.” He didn’t rule out the idea, but warned sanctions against Russian energy exports—a key part of Russia’s economy—could add fuel to the fire of the recent sharp rise in oil prices, an argument the Biden Administration has often articulated this week.

Earlier Friday, Bloomberg reported[https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2022-03-04/white-house-considering-ban-on-russian-oil-imports-to-u-s] the Biden Administration has discussed the possibility of a ban on Russian oil with both federal staffers and the petroleum industry, citing unnamed sources. But oil is fungible, and Bloomberg reports the administration is concerned any Russian crude oil that the United States stops importing may just go to other countries, causing U.S. prices to increase without any tangible impact on Russia’s bottom line.

Last year, the United States imported around 72.6 million[https://www.eia.gov/dnav/pet/hist/LeafHandler.ashx?n=PET&s=MCRIM_NUS-NRS_1&f=M] barrels of Russian crude oil, according to the U.S. Department of Energy’s statistics, a small fraction of the roughly 2.2 billion[https://www.eia.gov/dnav/pet/hist/LeafHandler.ashx?n=PET&s=MCRIMUS1&f=M] barrels of crude oil imported into the U.S. from all countries in the same year, with Canada, Mexico and Saudi Arabia ranking as the top foreign sources[https://www.eia.gov/petroleum/imports/companylevel/] of oil. (Russia accounted for about 20%[https://www.eia.gov/dnav/pet/hist/LeafHandler.ashx?n=PET&s=MTXIM_NUS-NRS_1&f=M] of the 861 million[https://www.eia.gov/dnav/pet/hist/LeafHandler.ashx?n=PET&s=MPEIMUS1&f=M] barrels of refined petroleum products the U.S. imported last year, but imports are small compared to domestic production.)

Even though sanctions from Western countries haven’t directly targeted oil imports, some of the other severe economic penalties imposed on Russia after it invaded Ukraine could disrupt the country’s energy sector. Russia has[https://www.marketwatch.com/story/why-russian-oil-cant-find-buyers-even-as-crude-nearly-touches-120-a-barrel-11646347974] reportedly[https://www.npr.org/2022/03/04/1084448619/russia-lowers-its-oil-prices-after-buyers-self-sanction] struggled[https://www.cnn.com/2022/03/03/investing/russia-oil-sanctions-ukraine/index.html] to find buyers for its oil, as traders steer clear of the country to avoid violating financial industry sanctions.

U.S. Officials Say Russian Attack On Power Plant A ‘War Crime’ That Almost Caused ‘Nuclear Catastrophe’

Linda Thomas-Greenfield said[https://www.cnn.com/europe/live-news/ukraine-russia-putin-news-03-04-22/h_c98b4ad678b4c0a67060d81796e15057] Friday that Russia nearly caused a "nuclear catastrophe" during an overnight attack that sparked a fire in the administration building at Europe's largest nuclear power plant.

Thomas-Greenfield called Russia's decision to attack the Zaporizhzhia nuclear power plant "madness" that threatened the security of both Ukrainians and Russians if there had been a meltdown and release of radiation.

The State Department also condemned the attack in a statement to Forbes. “The intentional targeting of civilians or civilian objects, including nuclear power plants, is a war crime, and we are assessing the circumstances of this operation,” a State Department spokesperson said. “But — regardless of the legality — this action was the height of irresponsibility, and the Kremlin must cease operations around nuclear infrastructure.”

Russian forces now control the facility after intense fighting overnight. The fire has been put out and the International Atomic Energy Agency hasn't found any evidence radioactive material was released into the environment.

Ukrainian workers running the plant have reportedly[https://www.thedailybeast.com/zaporizhzhia-nuclear-staff-working-at-gunpoint-after-russian-seizure-of-plant-says-owner] been forced to remain on the job at gunpoint.

UN Confirms 331 Civilian Deaths In Ukraine—But Real Number Likely To Be Much Higher

The United Nations said[https://www.ohchr.org/EN/NewsEvents/Pages/media.aspx?IsMediaPage=true] it had counted 331 civilian deaths and 675 civilian injuries in Ukraine since Russia’s invasion as of Thursday, adding the real number was likely to be higher.

The UN’s toll includes 312 adult deaths and 19 child deaths, and 644 injured adults and 31 injured children. It said the figures “could rise considerably further” as Russian attacks were delaying the verification of Ukrainian casualties, including the alleged "hundreds of civilian casualties” in the Ukrainian government-controlled area of Donetsk. Volnovakha was heavily damaged Thursday by Russian forces that used airstrikes and artillery shelling to attack the city, according[https://www.forbes.com/sites/katyasoldak/2022/03/03/thursday-march-3-russias-war-on-ukraine-news-and-information-from-ukraine/?sh=5509e47e3b5f] to Forbes Ukraine.

Most civilian casualties were caused by[https://www.ohchr.org/EN/NewsEvents/Pages/media.aspx?IsMediaPage=true] “explosive weapons with a wide impact area,” including shelling from heavy artillery, multi-launch rocket systems and missiles and airstrikes, the UN said.

The Ukrainian government puts the death toll far higher than the UN. Ukraine's emergency service said[https://www.reuters.com/world/more-than-2000-ukrainian-civilians-killed-during-russian-invasion-ukrainian-2022-03-02/] Wednesday more than 2,000 Ukrainian civilians had been killed since Russia’s invasion of the country last week.

March 4, 2022 5:09 PM EST

Russian Forces Remain Stalled Outside Kyiv As ‘Vast Majority’ Of $350 Million U.S. Aid Package Reaches Ukraine

Russian troops remain about 15 miles north of Ukraine’s capital city of Kyiv, as Ukrainian attacks combined with a destroyed bridge have slowed their advance, a senior U.S. defense official told reporters Friday. “We certainly believe that the Ukrainians blowing up that bridge absolutely had an effect on stopping and curtailing the movement of that convoy,” the official said. “But we also believe that they have hit the convoy at other places as well in direct attacks.”

Another senior U.S. defense official said about $240 million worth of aid from a $350 million U.S. security assistance package approved last week has been delivered to Ukraine, and the rest should arrive within the next few weeks. It’s the largest presidential drawdown package in history, according to the State Department, and it contains equipment Ukrainian forces have already been trained on. A total of 14 countries have provided security assistance to Ukraine since the invasion began, according to the official, who declined to specify the countries by name.

Among other updates:

* The Pentagon has “no reason to doubt” claims that Russia has taken control of the Zaporizhzhia nuclear power plant, according to the official, after Russian shelling sparked a fire near the plant overnight, which the first official described as “recklessness.”

* There is no evidence of radioactive leakage from the facility, all of the fires are out and the U.S. Department of Energy is monitoring the situation.

* Russian forces remain about 6 miles outside of the cities of Chernihiv and Kharkiv, though due to the urban sprawl of Kharkiv, the distance “equates to really being on the outskirts of the city.”

* The U.S. sees Russian forces having more success in the south, and is not disputing reports that Russia has taken Kherson, though there have been clashes and conflicts near the city.

* Mairupol remains under Ukrainian control, though the city is under heavy bombardment and Russian troops are continuing to advance, leading to utility outages and supply issues.

* There is also fighting near the city of Mykolaiv, northwest of Kherson.

* About 92% of Russian forces assembled for the invasion have entered Ukraine, up from 90% on Thursday.

* More than 500 Russian missiles have been launched since the invasion began, up from about 480 on Thursday.

* The airspace over Ukraine remains contested – “a significant majority” of Ukraine’s aircraft remain available, the official said, and Ukrainian air and missile defenses remain effective.

March 4, 2022 3:58 PM EST

Historically Neutral Switzerland Announces New Sanctions Against Russia

The Swiss government on Friday announced[https://www.admin.ch/gov/en/start/documentation/media-releases.msg-id-87474.html] a new round of sanctions targeting Russia, including banning all transactions involving the Russian Central Bank.

The sanctions mainly impact Russia's ability to secure financing and conduct international trade, such as suspending the use of public financing for investments in Russia and prohibiting the export of goods for use in Russia's oil and aerospace industries.

Switzerland took up the sanctions in line with penalties already approved by the European Union, including freezing the assets of dozens of Russian oligarchs[https://www.politico.eu/article/eu-sanctions-major-russia-oligarchs-rosneft-transneft/] with ties to Vladimir Putin.

The country on Tuesday closed its airspace to Russian flights as part of an earlier round[https://www.cnn.com/2022/03/01/business/switzerland-sanction-russia-freeze-assets-intl-hnk/index.html] of sanctions.

The Swiss government said in a statement it believes the sanctions are "compatible with Switzerland’s neutrality" while "due consideration is being given to humanitarian activities."

March 4, 2022 1:13 PM EST

U.S. Accuses Russia Of War Crime After Attack On Nuclear Power Plant

Russia’s attack on Europe’s largest nuclear power plant in Ukraine is a “war crime,” the U.S. embassy in Kyiv said[https://twitter.com/USEmbassyKyiv/status/1499708231903547394?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw%7Ctwcamp%5Etweetembed%7Ctwterm%5E1499708231903547394%7Ctwgr%5E%7Ctwcon%5Es1_&ref_url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cnn.com%2Feurope%2Flive-news%2Fukraine-russia-putin-news-03-04-22%2Findex.html] on Friday. “It is a war crime to attack a nuclear power plant,” the embassy tweeted, after Russian forces reportedly seized control of the Zaporizhzhia[https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/mar/04/zaporizhzhia-nuclear-power-plant-everything-you-need-to-know] power plant.

The embassy added: “Putin's shelling of Europe's largest nuclear plant takes his reign of terror one step further” and Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky accused[https://www.telegraph.co.uk/world-news/2022/03/04/russia-ukraine-nuclear-plant-fire-radiation-zaporizhzhia/] Russia of nuclear terrorism. In addition to Zaporizhzhia, Russia has also seized the Chernobyl site.

The accusation by a U.S. entity goes a step further than previous criticisms of Russian aggression in Ukraine, which have alluded to, but stopped short[https://edition.cnn.com/2022/03/02/politics/biden-ukraine/index.html] of, explicit accusations of war crimes. Russia has been accused of using prohibited weapons like vacuum bombs and cluster munitions in Ukraine and of intentionally targeting civilians.

A fire broke out at the plant overnight Friday when a Russian projectile hit the complex, sparking fears of a nuclear incident and international condemnation. Three Ukrainian troops were killed and two security staff at the plant injured, according to Ukraine’s state nuclear company and the UN’s nuclear watchdog, which said the projectile did not hit a reactor and no radioactive material had been released. However, the situation “remains very challenging,” said International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA) chief Rafael Grossi.

March 4, 2022 12:02 PM EST

UN Human Rights Council Backs Probe Into Russian Invasion Of Ukraine

The United Nations Human Rights Council on Friday voted[https://www.jpost.com/international/article-699342] overwhelmingly to create[https://twitter.com/trussliz/status/1499704214129463297?s=20&t=kD1Q3ZAI6A31R0Ts9APfow] a high-level investigation into alleged human rights violations committed during Russia’s invasion of Ukraine. Thirty-two out of the council’s 47 members backed the probe, which aims to hold responsible parties accountable if violations have occurred. Russia—which denies committing any human rights violations like targeting civilians in Ukraine—was one of two countries to vote against the investigation. Eritrea also voted against establishing the probe, and remaining countries including India, Kazakhstan, Sudan and Cuba, abstained. Ukrainian President Volodymyr Zelensky praised the decision and said “Russian war criminals will be held accountable.”

States are elected to serve three year terms on the UNHRC by members of the UN’s General Assembly. The council’s inclusion of countries accused of human rights violations is controversial and widely criticized by Western governments. Russia is no exception and rights groups pointed[https://www.themoscowtimes.com/2020/10/14/russia-returns-to-un-human-rights-council-after-4-year-absence-a71746] to alleged war crimes before it was reelected in 2020. Russia’s membership on the council has become another flashpoint following its invasion of Ukraine, where it has been accused of targeting civilians, nuclear facilities and using prohibited weapons including cluster munitions[https://www.abc.net.au/news/2022-03-01/ukraine-ambassador-us-russia-used-vacuum-bomb-cluster-munitions/100870638] and vacuum[https://www.dw.com/en/is-russia-using-vacuum-bombs-in-ukraine/a-61001817] bombs.

Several Russian banks have already[https://www.forbes.com/sites/lisakim/2022/02/26/us-and-allies-to-remove-some-russian-banks-from-swift-sanction-central-bank/?sh=1e0bf8ad165a] been cut off from SWIFT in earlier[https://www.reuters.com/business/finance/eu-excludes-seven-russian-banks-swift-official-journal-2022-03-02/] waves of sanctions and Coveney did not specify which banks are set to be barred.

Coveney said the bloc will be “significantly adding” to its sanctions on Russia today, which are about “isolating” the country on the international stage, and he expects it will ban imports like steel, timber, aluminum and potentially even coal, noting the latter could have an impact on already volatile fuel prices in Europe. Russian flagged ships will also likely be banned from entering EU ports, Coveney added.

"These are big decisions by the European Union that are going to have an impact on Ireland and EU countries,” Coveney said. “But they’re decisions that need to be taken to send very, very strong messages to Russia that this madness must stop."

IAEA Says ‘No Release’ Of Radioactive Material After Attack On Europe’s Largest Nuclear Plant

Attacks on Ukraine’s Zaporizhzhia nuclear power plant have not damaged any of the site’s six nuclear reactors and there has been no release of radioactive material, said International Atomic Energy Agency head Rafael Grossi at a press conference Friday. Five of the plant’s reactors are offline, Grossi added, with the remaining reactor operating at 60% capacity. Safety systems were “not affected at all” in the attack, which hit an “adjacent” building. Two security personnel died in the attack, Grossi said.

Grossi added the situation is “fragile” and “unstable,” and offered to personally travel to Chernobyl, which is under the control[https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-us-canada-60514228] of Russian forces, in order to speak with Russia and Ukraine. The agency head stressed the initiative is about ensuring the safety of nuclear operations and has “nothing to do” with the political situation between Russia and Ukraine as this is beyond the IAEA’s remit. “I am not a self-appointed mediator.”

BBC, Radio Liberty Restricted In Russia

Russia’s censorship of the war intensified Friday after the country’s communications regulator blocked access to the BBC’s local website and Radio Liberty.

According to GlobalCheck, which tracks site outages and availability, Twitter and Facebook were also restricted in Russia as of Friday morning, while Radio Liberty reports[https://www.rferl.org/a/russia-rferl-bbc-facebook-google-twitter-blocked/31735597.html] that Google’s Play Store and Apple’s App Store were also blocked.

Russia’s domestic coverage of the war is largely sanitized and independent broadcasters[https://eeas.europa.eu/headquarters/headquarters-homepage/112128/russia-statement-high-representative-josep-borrell-media-censorship-and-crackdown-peaceful_en] have been taken off air[https://theintercept.com/2022/03/02/russian-tv-ukraine-war-conspiracy/] over the past week.

Russian state-backed channel RT came off air in the U.K. and EU Thursday and CNN reported the channel’s U.S. arm was shutting down[https://edition.cnn.com/2022/03/03/media/rt-america-layoffs/index.html] the same day.

Stocks In Asia, Europe Drop

Stocks in Asia slid Friday and in pre-market trading in Europe as investors react to the ongoing impact of Russia’s invasion of Ukraine.

Hong Kong’s Hang Seng index and Tokyo’s Nikkei were down more than 2%, while stocks in Shanghai fell almost 1%.

The drop comes after a blaze broke out at Europe’s largest nuclear plant in Zaporizhzhia, southeastern Ukraine, following shelling by Russian forces, but was later extinguished and no deaths were reported.

Benchmarks in London and across Europe were down around 2% in pre-market trading.

Moscow’s stock exchange will remain closed[https://www.wsj.com/livecoverage/russia-ukraine-latest-news-2022-03-04/card/russia-holds-off-again-on-reopening-stock-market-FMAptR8yuRRluLx6fVVI] for a fifth day Friday. Regulators in the U.S. along with the London Stock Exchange also halted trading with dozens of firms linked to Russia this week.

March 4, 2022 1:03 AM EST

Fire Reported At Ukraine's Zaporizhzhia Nuclear Plant As Russian Troops Approach

A fire has broken out at the Zaporizhzhia Nuclear Power Plant—Ukraine’s largest nuclear plant—amid intense fighting with nearby Russian forces, local and national officials said early Friday morning.

Ukrainian Foreign Minister Dmytro Kuleba tweeted[https://twitter.com/DmytroKuleba/status/1499543775240196099] Russia is “firing from all sides upon Zaporizhzhia,” causing a fire to start, and Dmytro Orlov—the mayor of nearby Energodar—confirmed the fire on social media, according to Reuters[https://www.reuters.com/markets/europe/top-wrap-1-europes-largest-nuclear-power-plant-fire-after-russian-attack-mayor-2022-03-04/]. Orlov and Kuleba didn’t offer further details on the extent of the fire.

Russian ground troops are fighting within several kilometers of the nuclear plant and are “moving directly towards” the site, Ukraine’s nuclear regulator told the International Atomic Energy Agency, a situation Ukrainian officials called “critical.” The IAEA said in a press release[https://www.iaea.org/newscenter/pressreleases/update-9-iaea-director-general-statement-on-the-situation-in-ukraine] the Russian military “broke through the block-post” outside the town of Energodar, and fighting is now ongoing in the town and on the road to the nuclear plant. On Wednesday, a crowd of civilians[https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/2022/03/03/zaporizhzhia-nuclear-plant-blockade-drone-video/] had blocked Russian troops from entering Energodar.

The Zaporizhzhia Nuclear Power Plant is the second-largest nuclear plant[https://www.forbes.com/sites/craighooper/2022/03/03/russia-about-to-take-6-nuclear-reactors-no-plan-to-safely-operate-them/?sh=33e3423164ac] in Europe, and one of the 10 largest in the world, with six pressurized water reactors. Last week, Russian forces also captured Chernobyl, the site of a massive nuclear disaster in 1986.

March 3, 2022 11:08 PM EST

U.S. Offers Temporary Immigration Protections To Ukrainians Amid Russian War

The U.S. Department of Homeland Security will offer Temporary Protected Status to Ukrainians who entered the United States by March 1, allowing them to temporarily remain in the country without fear of deportation, DHS said Thursday[https://www.dhs.gov/news/2022/03/03/secretary-mayorkas-designates-ukraine-temporary-protected-status-18-months]. The move comes as Russia’s invasion of Ukraine has forced more than 1 million Ukrainians to flee their home country.

U.S. Immigration and Customs Enforcement has also paused deportation flights to Ukraine, a spokesperson for the agency told Forbes earlier Thursday.

Temporary Protected Status allows people from countries devastated by wars or natural disasters to temporarily live and work in the United States, even after their current U.S. visa expires. TPS for Ukraine will last 18 months, DHS said, though the Biden Administration has the power to extend it. The Migration Policy Institute, a think tank, has[https://www.nytimes.com/2022/02/28/world/europe/biden-ukraine-visas.html] reportedly[https://www.cnn.com/europe/live-news/ukraine-russia-putin-news-03-03-22/index.html] estimated[https://www.nytimes.com/2022/02/28/world/europe/biden-ukraine-visas.html] there are about 30,000 Ukrainians in the United States who are neither U.S. citizens or permanent residents, making them potentially eligible for Temporary Protected Status.

Deportations of Ukrainians are fairly unusual: ICE removed just 106 Ukrainian citizens from the United States in fiscal year 2020, a tiny share of the agency’s 185,884 total deportations[https://www.forbes.com/sites/joewalsh/2020/12/23/ice-deportations-and-arrests-plummeted-in-2020-heres-why/?sh=60fe79d026a7] that year. Some 125 Ukrainians were deported in 2019 and 105 were deported in 2018.

A dozen other countries racked by conflict or natural disaster are currently designated for Temporary Protected Status, including Haiti and Syria.

Ukrainian presidential advisor Mykhailo Podolyak tweeted the tentative deal was made during a round of talks between Russian and Ukrainian officials on Thursday, though he added no agreement was made on Ukraine’s top priorities—a nationwide ceasefire and armistice.

“The results Ukraine needs are not yet achieved,” Podolyak tweeted.

March 3, 2022 5:40 PM EST

U.S. Says 90% Of Russian Forces Are Now In Ukraine As Troops Remain ‘Largely Stalled’ In The North

Russia has directed about 90% of the combat power it assembled for its invasion across the border and into Ukraine, a senior U.S. defense official said during a press briefing Thursday. Ukrainian cities to the north and east remain under heavy bombardment a week into the invasion, though Russian forces are “largely stalled” in northern Ukraine.

Here are additional updates from the senior defense official:

* More than 480 Russian missile launches have been fired since the war began, with the majority coming from inside Ukraine, followed by Russia (more than 160), Belarus (more than 70) and the Black Sea (less than 10).

* The Pentagon cannot independently verify reports[https://www.nytimes.com/2022/03/02/world/europe/kherson-ukraine-russia.html] that the city of Kherson is under Russian control, as both sides are “still fighting.”

* Russian forces are on the outskirts of Kyiv to the north and northwest, and the 40-mile[http://www.forbes.com/sites/forbesstaffreports/2022/03/01/live-russia-ukraine-invasion/] military convoy remains stalled.

* The senior defense official said Russian troops appear to be trying to get to Kyiv “every single day” and are facing strong Ukrainian resistance, along with supply issues, as they intend to encircle the city.

* None of the Russian forces outside of Kyiv are closer than 25 kilometers, or about 15 miles, from the city.

* There is increasing bombardment in Chernihiv and Kharkiv, and there are indications Russian troops are “just outside the city” in Kharkiv.

* Russian forces are advancing on Mariupol from the north and from the coastline “with the apparent intention to isolate the city.”

* The airspace over Ukraine remains “contested,” and Ukrainian air defenses remain intact and effective.

— Anna Kaplan

Russia Reportedly Considering Public Executions To Break Ukrainian Morale

Russia has drafted plans to crush the morale of the Ukrainian people in captured cities that include imprisoning political opponents and potentially public executions, according to Bloomberg[https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2022-03-03/russia-attacks-cities-as-war-enters-second-week-ukraine-update?srnd=premium-europe], which cites a European intelligence official.

Lukoil, Russia's Second-Biggest Company, Calls For Peace Talks

Russia's second-largest company has requested peace talks to end the war in Ukraine, according to multiple[https://twitter.com/AFP/status/1499410431726731271?s=20&t=lxD8hi76XxLlcFDvUwqQfQ] reports[https://www.barrons.com/news/russian-oil-giant-lukoil-calls-for-halt-to-ukraine-war-bur-har-01646322607?refsec=afp-news].

Lukoil, which is led by Russian billionaire Vagit Alekperov[https://www.forbes.com/profile/vagit-alekperov/?sh=32c30a883a74], joined the calls for peace Thursday after boycott[https://www.cnn.com/2022/03/01/business/russia-lukoil-boycott/index.html] threats against its gas stations in the U.S., though most are owned by Americans as franchises.

The company's London-listed stock[https://www.cnbc.com/2022/03/03/london-listed-russian-stocks-are-collapsing-with-trading-now-suspended.html] has plummeted more than 99% since Russia invaded Ukraine last week.

Alekperov's net worth has declined to around $18.6 billion after peaking at nearly $25 billion last year, according to Forbes.

Mariupol Mayor Says Russian Forces Created ‘Humanitarian Disaster’ In City

The mayor of Mariupol, a city in southeastern Ukraine that is surrounded[https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2022/world/europe/ukraine-maps.html?name=styln-russia-ukraine®ion=TOP_BANNER&block=storyline_menu_recirc&action=click&pgtype=LegacyCollection&variant=0_Control&is_new=false] by Russian forces, said Russian soldiers had destroyed bridges and created a “blockade,” trapping women, children and the elderly in the city. “We are being destroyed as a nation,” Mayor Vadym Boychenko wrote[https://m.facebook.com/1547688482223955/posts/3178884925770961] in a Facebook post. “This is the genocide of the Ukrainian people.”

Boychenko said Russian troops “deliberately destroyed” the city’s infrastructure for the past seven days, and that the city is working to restore infrastructure capabilities. Boychenko also called on international groups to create a humanitarian corridor to help get supplies to the city to aid the “humanitarian disaster.”

March 3, 2022 2:22 PM EST

Biden Wants $10 Billion More In Aid For Ukraine, Report Says

The Biden administration will ask Congress to approve an additional $10 billion in aid for Ukraine, the Washington Post reports[https://www.washingtonpost.com/us-policy/2022/03/03/ukraine-covid-aid-white-house-request/], citing two anonymous sources familiar with the plans. The $10 billion request aims to address the growing humanitarian crisis in the country, supporting Ukraine’s defenses, protecting the country’s electrical grid and assisting other European allies, the Post reports.

Lawmakers have been working in recent weeks to create a long-term deal to fund the government, as the spending bill is set to expire[https://www.forbes.com/sites/zacharysmith/2022/02/17/senate-passes-last-minute-bill-to-prevent-government-shutdown/] on March 11. Senate Minority Leader Mitch McConnell (R-Ky.) said[https://www.c-span.org/video/?c5003841/senate-republican-leader-omnibus-spending-bill-talks-have-hit-snag] there had been a “snag” over Ukraine assistance, claiming Democrats were trying to take the money from funds they had planned to allocate to the Pentagon.

The Post reports the Biden administration’s new request may alleviate these concerns, as the funding would come from new emergency aid for Ukraine, not repurposed dollars for the Department of Defense.

March 3, 2022 1:33 PM EST

UN Nuclear Watchdog Urges Russia To Cease Action At Ukrainian Nuclear Sites

The International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA) passed a resolution condemning Russia’s invasion of Ukraine on Thursday and called[https://twitter.com/AFP/status/1499356191633383431?s=20&t=6bCVEUw-PwZUI6tPWFk29g] on Russia to “immediately cease actions” at Ukrainian nuclear sites, according to AFP and Reuters[https://www.reuters.com/world/europe/iaea-board-deplores-russian-invasion-ukraine-only-two-votes-against-2022-03-03/]. Twenty six countries on the board of the UN’s nuclear watchdog supported the resolution, five abstained (India, Pakistan, Senegal, Vietnam and South Africa) and two (Mexico and Burundi) were absent. Just two voted against the resolution: Russia and China.

The resolution comes amid reports[https://www.theguardian.com/world/2022/mar/03/russian-forces-surround-ukraines-biggest-nuclear-plant-sparking-un-concerns] Russian forces have surrounded Ukraine’s biggest nuclear plant—the largest in Europe—and a day after IAEA chief Rafael Grossi warned[https://edition.cnn.com/europe/live-news/ukraine-russia-putin-news-03-02-22/h_b5223142c78cee998ed02780fe44a605] about the dangers of war around nuclear facilities. Russian forces have already taken control[https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/2022/02/24/ukraine-russia-chernobyl-risk/] over the decommissioned Chernobyl power plant, the site of the world’s worst nuclear disaster.

The move follows Sberbank’s exit from Europe on Tuesday and a barrage of Western sanctions against Russia that has cracked down on financial institutions, Putin allies and oligarchs.

March 3, 2022 9:55 AM EST

Russian And Belarusian Athletes Banned From Winter Paralympic Games

Russian and Belarusian athletes have been banned from the Beijing 2022 Paralympic Winter Games, the International Paralympic Committee announced[https://www.paralympic.org/news/ipc-decline-athlete-entries-rpc-and-npc-belarus-beijing-2022] Thursday. The move comes a day after the sporting body faced widespread criticism and threats of boycotts following it’s initial decision[https://www.forbes.com/sites/roberthart/2022/03/02/winter-paralympics-russian-and-belarusian-athletes-to-compete-as-neutrals-in-beijing/?sh=38d701bc3545] to allow athletes from those countries to compete under a neutral flag.

The number of athletes, teams and national Paralympic committees threatening to boycott the Games over Russian and Belarusian involvement was jeopardizing the “viability” of the Games, IPC president Andrew Parsons said in a statement[https://www.paralympic.org/news/ipc-decline-athlete-entries-rpc-and-npc-belarus-beijing-2022] explaining the u-turn.

The “situation in the athlete villages is escalating” and has become “untenable,” putting the IPC in a “unique and impossible position” close to the Games’ launch on Friday, Parsons added.

March 3, 2022 9:45 AM EST

Biden Set To Meet Other Leaders Of The Quad Alliance Later Today

Leaders of the Indo-Pacific security alliance known as the Quad—including U.S. President Joe Biden—will hold a virtual meeting[https://apnews.com/article/russia-ukraine-united-nations-general-assembly-joe-biden-india-australia-6fd7660d92ad81512a231e81628d46a6?utm_source=Twitter&utm_campaign=SocialFlow&utm_medium=AP] on Thursday, the group’s first meeting since Russia’s invasion of Ukraine.

A statement[https://www.mea.gov.in/press-releases.htm?dtl/34924/Quad_Leaders_Virtual_meeting_on_3March_2022] put out by the Indian Foreign Ministry noted that the leaders in the meeting will “exchange views and assessments about important developments in the Indo-Pacific,” with no mention of the Russia-Ukraine situation.

The Quad—which Beijing has referred to as Indo-Pacific NATO—is made up of the U.S., India, Australia and Japan and is seen as counterbalance against China.

On the issue of Russia, however, the groups appears to be divided with Japan and Australia backing the U.S. and NATO’s condemnation of the invasion of Ukraine, while India has chosen to remain on the sideline and has abstained from key votes on the issue in the U.N.

Spotify Closes Russian Office, Blames 'unprovoked attack on Ukraine'

Streaming giant Spotify shut down its Russian office indefinitely on Wednesday citing Moscow’s “unprovoked attack on Ukraine,” according to[https://www.reuters.com/technology/spotify-closes-its-office-russia-response-attack-ukraine-2022-03-02/] Reuters.

“Our first priority over the past week has been the safety of our employees and to ensure that Spotify continues to serve as an important source of global and regional news at a time when access to information is more important than ever,” Spotify said in a statement. The company also noted that it has reviewed thousands of pieces of content since the start of the war and has limited the discoverability of shows run by Russian state-affiliated media.

Spotify joins a growing list of tech companies that have taken action against Russia and its state-sponsored media following its invasion of Ukraine.

March 3, 2022 6:37 AM EST

U.S. State Department Criticizes Russia's Media Clampdown

The US State Department accused[https://www.state.gov/media-crackdown-in-russia/] Moscow of launching a “full assault on media freedom and the truth” as various independent news outlets in Russia faced a government crackdown.

Earlier this week, the Russian Prosecutor General blocked access to two independent media outlets, Radio Ekho Moskvy and Dozhd TV, accusing them of reporting "false" information about the invasion. This followed moves by the communications regulator Roskomnadzor last week to restrict access to major social media platforms including Twitter, Facebook, and Instagram.

“The people of Russia did not choose this war. Putin did. They have a right to know about the death, suffering and destruction being inflicted by their government on the people of Ukraine. The people of Russia also have a right to know about the human costs of this senseless war to their own soldiers.”- U.S. State Department Spokesperson Ned Price

The State Department called on Vladimir Putin and the Russian government to immediately “cease this bloodshed” and withdraw troops from Ukraine.

March 3, 2022 6:09 AM EST

New Delhi Denies Russian Claims That Indian Students Are Being Held Hostage In Ukraine

The Indian government on Thursday denied a Russian allegation that Indian students stranded in Ukraine were being held hostages and used as “human shields” by the Ukrainian military.

On Wednesday, following a telephone conversation between Russian President Vladimir Putin and Indian Prime Minister Narendra Modi, the Kremlin has issued a statement noting that Putin had warned Modi about Indians students being taken hostage.

On Thursday, however, Indian foreign ministry spokesperson Arindam Bagchi stated[https://twitter.com/MEAIndia/status/1499221560657252356] that India had not “received any reports of any hostage situation regarding any student.” Bagchi added that many Indian students were able to leave Kharkiv on Wednesday “with the cooperation of Ukrainian authorities.”

Click to access link.[https://embedly.forbes.com/widgets/media.html?type=text%2Fhtml&key=3ce26dc7e3454db5820ba084d28b4935&schema=twitter&url=https%3A//twitter.com/meaindia/status/1499221560657252356&image=https%3A//i.embed.ly/1/image%3Furl%3Dhttps%253A%252F%252Fabs.twimg.com%252Ferrors%252Flogo46x38.png%26key%3D3ce26dc7e3454db5820ba084d28b4935]

Earlier this week, shelling in Kharkiv claimed the life of one Indian student who had been standing in a queue to buy food. The student’s death led to widespread criticism of the Indian government by opposition politicians who accused it of failing to safely evacuate Indians from the war zone. According to New Delhi, more than 17,000 Indians have been evacuated from Ukraine as of Wednesday with a few thousand more remaining.

Despite its growing security partnership with the West, India has abstained from criticizing Russia’s invasion and has called for a diplomatic resolution to the situation. Amid growing tensions with its neighbor China both along its Himalayan mountain border and in the Indian Ocean, New Delhi has developed close security ties with the U.S. and its allies but still remains heavily reliant on Russian-made weaponry.
